<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Second Tithe is to be used for eating, drinking, and anointing; to eat everything commonly eaten, to anoint with what commonly is used for anointing. One should not anoint with wine or vinegar, but with olive oil<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">These two sentences are stated, for Sabbatical produce and money, in Mishnah <i>Ševi‘it</i> 8:2.</i>. One does not use Second Tithe oil as base for perfume<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Since then the oil is no longer food.</i>, one does not buy perfumed oil with Second Tithe money<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Even though anointing with Second Tithe oil is permitted, one may buy only what is used by the majority of people, not what is reserved for a minority of the rich and pampered.</i>, but one may make spice wine. If one adds it to honey and spices and it increases in value, the increase is proportional<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">If some dish offered for sale in Jerusalem was prepared with Second Tithe material worth <i>a</i>, and profane material worth <i>b</i>, and now the dish is worth <i>c &gt; a + b</i>, then <i>cb/(a+b)</i> is profane and may be paid with Second Tithe money; the rest must be paid with profane money.</i>. If fish is cooked with Second Tithe leeks which increases its value, the increase is proportional. If a dough of Second Tithe when baked increased in value, the increase belongs to the Second<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Since the profane fuel did not change the body of the bread, the entire bread made from Second Tithe flour has the status of Second Tithe.</i>. This is the principle: In all cases in which the increase is visible<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of this expression will be discussed in the Halakhah.</i>, the increase is proportional. In all cases in which the increase is not visible, the increase belongs to the Second.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following paragraphs (up to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 2:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.2.1.9">Note 35</a>) are also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 8:3:2-15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.8.3.2-15"><i>Yoma</i> 8:3</a> (fol. 45a), <i>Ševu‘ot</i> 3:2 (fol. 34b). The parallel discussion in the Babli is <i>Ševu‘ot</i> 22b–23a.</i>“Second Tithe is to be used for eating,” etc. It is to be used for eating, since eating is written regarding it<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:23" href="/Deuteronomy.14.23"><i>Deut</i>. 14:23</a>.</i>. For drinking, since drinking is included in eating. From where that drinking is included in eating? Rebbi Jonah understood if from the following (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 17:12" href="/Leviticus.17.12"><i>Lev</i>. 17:12</a>): “Therefore, I said to the Children of Israel, no person among you may eat blood.” Where do we hold? If about congealed blood, did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Tahorot 2:2-3" href="/Tosefta_Tahorot.2.2-3">Tosephta <i>Tahorot</i> 2:5</a>.</i>: “Congealed blood is neither food nor drink”? So we must hold as is<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Fluid blood.</i>, and the Torah called it “eating.” But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 120a" href="/Chullin.120a">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 120a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 21a" href="/Menachot.21a"><i>Menaḥot</i> 21a</a>.</i>: “If he mashed the fat<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The fat of domestic animals which from sacrifices is burned on the altar and from profane meat is forbidden as food.</i> and sipped it, congealed the blood and ate it, he is guilty!” How does Rebbi Jonah explain? It is neither food, to accept the impurity of food, nor drink, to accept the impurity of drinks<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The Tosephta <i>Tahorot</i> adds explicitly: “If he thought of [the congealed blood] as food, it accepts the impurities of food.” The argument of R. Jonah is not acceptable.</i>.
Rebbi Jonah changed his mind and understood it from the following (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:26" href="/Deuteronomy.14.26"><i>Deut</i>. 14:26</a>): “Spend the money for anything you desire<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is from the later part of the verse: “cattle and sheep, wine and liquor, anything you might want, and eat it there before the Eternal, your God, …” How does one eat wine and liquor?</i>.” Where do we hold? If one adds the taste of wine to a dish, is that not tasting badly<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Ševu‘ot</i> and <i>Yoma</i>, the argument closes with: So we must hold as is, and the Torah called it “eating,” as for the first argument.</i>? The rabbis of Caesarea say, explain it with <i>’rznyyh</i> and <i>gmzvzynyyh</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Yoma</i>אורזנייא וגוממני, in <i>Ševu‘ot</i> גמרייה ואורזרייה. Every commentator and author of a dictionary has his own identification. {Perhaps cf. Greek spiced liquor <i>Retsina</i>, classical Greek ῥητίνα “<i>pine resin</i>”, and various kinds of gum (<i>gummi</i>, κόμμι) used in manufacture of fine liquors. (E. G.)} The variants make a reliable identification impossible. The additions involved must be such that wine (or liquor) is an essential ingredient of the recipe. In that case, one cannot say that a dish made with wine tastes worse than wine itself. R. Yose has not proved that from Second Tithe money one may buy wine other than cooking wine.</i>. Anything auxiliary to food is considered food.
<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The text is difficult. The parallel in <i>Ševu‘ot</i> is almost unintelligible. The best text seems to be the one in <i>Yoma</i> {commentary in braces}:<br>Rebbi Yose understood it from the following (Mishnah <i>Ševu‘ot</i> 3:2): “An oath {meaning: I swear} that I shall not eat, when he ate or drank he is guilty on one count only.” {This proves the point.} The colleagues said before Rebbi Yose, one continues after this {in the same Mishnah}, “an oath that I shall neither eat nor drink, when he ate and drank he is guilty on two counts.” {This seems to disprove that drinking is subsumed under eating.} Rebbi Yose said to them, is that not as if somebody had two loaves before him when he said: an oath that I shall not eat this one, and then he said: an oath that I shall not eat that one, would he not be guilty on two counts? {Therefore, drinking in general is subsumed under eating but if somebody spells them out separately, he takes upon himself two parallel but separate obligations.}</i> Rebbi Yose understood it from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">This is a misquote from Mishnah <i>Ševu‘ot</i> 3:2; the correct text is given by the colleagues. The second quote by R. Yose is correct.</i>: “[I swear] an oath that I shall not eat, when he ate and drank he is guilty on two counts.” The colleagues said, he is guilty only on one count. Rebbi Yose said to them, we say after this, “an oath that I shall neither eat nor drink, when he ate and drank he is guilty on two counts.” It is as if somebody had two loaves before him when he said: [I swear] an oath that I shall not eat this one, and then he said: [I swear] an oath that I shall not eat that one, would he not be guilty on two counts?
Rebbi Ḥanania in the name of Rebbi Phineas understood it from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ševu‘ot</i> 3:2.</i>: “[I swear] an oath that I shall not eat, when he ate inedible food and drank undrinkable fluids he is free from punishment.” Therefore, if he drank drinkable fluids he is guilty. That is correct following “an oath that I shall not eat,” and in our Mishnah they teach “an oath that I shall not eat.” But for those rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The anonymous editors of the Babli, <i>Ševu‘ot</i> 23a, to show that from the Mishnah one cannot infer that drinking is a subcategory of eating.</i> who teach “an oath that I shall not eat and I shall not drink”?
Rebbi Ḥinena understood it from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 8:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.8.3.1">Mishnah <i>Yoma</i> 8:3</a>. A person who forgot that it was the Day of Atonement and he ate or drank must bring a sin sacrifice. But if he ate and drank in one forgetting episode he brings only one sacrifice. The argument is weak since the commandment of the Day of Atonement is “to deprive oneself”, and not eating and not drinking are aspects of deprivation.</i>: “If he ate and drank in one forgetting he is guilty only once.” Rebbi Abba Mari understood it from here (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:14" href="/Deuteronomy.26.14"><i>Deut</i>. 26:14</a>): “I did not eat from it in my mourning”, but I drank<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">From the declaration the farmer makes in the Temple when he brings his Second Tithe to Jerusalem after he gave First Tithe and, if applicable, the tithe of the poor. It seems obvious that the declaration cannot be made if the farmer drank from tithe wine while in deep mourning, which is a desecration as if he had eaten. Therefore, the verse subsumes drinking under eating.</i>?
It is understandable<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The text in <i>Ševu‘ot</i> which reads עד כדון “so far”, probably is better. The entire paragraph belongs to the Mishnah in <i>Ševu‘ot</i>; here ends the parallel in <i>Ševu‘ot</i>.</i> for him who said, “[I swear] an oath that I shall not eat”, and he drank<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The preceding argument shows that drinking is subsumed under eating. But everybody agrees that a mention of drink does not include solid food. The same conclusion is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 23a" href="/Shevuot.23a">Babli <i>Ševu‘ot</i> 23a</a>.</i>. But for him who said, “an oath that I shall not drink”, and he ate? Drinking is subsumed under eating, but eating is not subsumed under drinking.
Some want to understand it from this (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:17" href="/Deuteronomy.12.17"><i>Deut</i>. 12:17</a>): “You may not eat in your gates the tithe of your grain, your cider, and your shining oil.”<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Both drinking and anointing are subsumed under “eating”.</i> ‘Your cider’, that is the wine. ‘And your shining oil’ refers to anointing and the Torah called it ‘eating’. But this is not clear. If it were clear one should be whipped because of it outside the walls<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">If the verse were a formal identification of anointing and eating as far as Second Tithe is concerned, the use of impure heave oil for anointing should be a criminal offense.</i>! Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, one is whipped outside the walls only for pure Second Tithe which entered Jerusalem and left<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">This amoraïc statement is part of the objection.</i>. From where that it is not clear? From what was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The text from here to the end of the next paragraph is only hinted at in <i>Yoma</i>; it is in <i>Šabbat</i> 9:4, fol. 12a–b, the fullest text in <i>Ta‘aniot</i> 1:6, fol. 64c.</i>: “On the Sabbath, both anointing for pleasure and anointing not for pleasure are permitted. On the Day of Atonement, both anointing for pleasure and anointing not for pleasure are forbidden. On the Ninth of Ab and public fasts<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Fasts in a winter of draught, whose rules are modeled on those for the Ninth of Ab.</i> anointing for pleasure is forbidden but anointing not for pleasure is permitted.” Did we not state: “Anointing is equal to drinking for prohibition and replacement<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Referring to illegal use of heave and dedicated food by non-Cohanim and its replacement by 5/4 of the value taken.</i> but not for punishment, on the Day of Atonement for prohibition but not for punishment<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The only biblical prohibitions on the Day of Atonement are eating, drinking, and working. The other two, anointing and sexual relations, are rabbinic and not subject to biblical punishment.</i>”? Did we not state (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:15" href="/Leviticus.22.15"><i>Lev</i>. 22:15</a>): “They should not desecrate,” to include him who anoints and him who drinks<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The verse refers to the non-Cohen who “eats” holy food in error.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, there is no ‘anoints’ here. Rebbi Abba Mari said, if there is no ‘anoints’ here then there is no ‘drinks’ for otherwise something that comes from two different prohibitions would be added together<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">If the verse in <i>Lev</i>. is needed to include drinking in eating then it is incomprehensible that for inadvertently eating and drinking together on the Day of Atonement one should be responsible only for one sacrifice since in that case, one infringes on two separate biblical prohibitions and should be responsible for two separate sacrifices. Similarly, if one illegimately ate and drank heave one should be responsible for two separate fifths. Since in both cases the Mishnah treats eating and drinking together, the verse cannot express a separate status for drinking; the addition of anointing and drinking is rabbinic interpretation but not biblical law and there is no reason to exclude anointing.</i>!
From where that it is clear as a positive commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">While illegitimate use of heave oil for anointing is a prohibition, it is mentioned in the framework of the farmer’s declaration in the Temple which is a positive commandment. Overstepping the prohibition of anointing when it is forbidden is legally overstepping a positive commandment not under the scope of biblical penal law.</i>? Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Simai (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:14" href="/Deuteronomy.26.14"><i>Deut</i>. 26:14</a>): “Nor did I give from it to the dead.” Where do we hold? If it were to bring a casket and shrouds for him, that were also forbidden for a living person<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Only consuming Second Tithe is permitted.</i>! If something is forbidden for the living, not so much more for the dead? What is something which is permitted for the living but prohibited for the dead? That is anointing!
<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The text, with minor orthographic changes, is identical with the text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 8:2:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.8.2.2-3"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 8:2, Notes 22–28</a>. Since the Mishnah here does not have the sentence “to drink everything one commonly drinks” in practically all mss., the text in <i>Ševi‘it</i> is original.</i> What means “to eat everything commonly eaten”? “One does not oblige anybody to eat mouldy bread, or discarded leaves of vegetables, or spoiled food.” On the other hand, if somebody wants to eat raw beets or chew raw wheat kernels one does not listen to him. What means “to drink everything one commonly drinks”? “One does not oblige anybody to drink oily fish sauce, or sour fish sauce, or wine with yeast. He who has a toothache should not sip vinegar and spit out but he may sip and swallow. He may dip his food in as much [vinegar] as he wants without second thoughts. He who has a throat ache should not gargle with oil but he may load fish sauce with oil and sip it.” “One should not anoint with wine or vinegar, but with olive oil.” “If somebody has a headache or he has a scab, he may rub with oil but not with wine or vinegar.”
Perfumed wine of Second Tithe may not be used for anointing. Perfumed oil of Second Tithe may be used for anointing. What is the difference? For the latter it is customary, for the former it is not customary.
Rebbi Yudan asked: Second tithe oil that became rancid? Rebbi Mana said, when it became rancid, its holiness burst from it. Why did he have to ask? Sabbatical [oil] even if rancid remains in its holiness<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">At least as long as it still is animal feed. Second Tithe may not be used for animal feed.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The paragraph is also in <i>Šabbat</i>14:3, fol. 14c.</i> Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: He who whispers<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">A medical procedure where rubbing with oil is accompanied by whispered recitation of charms. The Talmud in <i>Šabbat</i> (Note 39) accepts this procedure for illnesses of the eye, intestines, and snake and scorpion bites. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 101a" href="/Sanhedrin.101a">Babli (<i>Sanhedrin</i> 101a</a>) approves whispered charms only for snake and scorpion bites; for eye and intestinal problems they approve only anointing and wrappings.</i> puts oil on his head and whispers on condition that he use neither hand nor vessel<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">These rules are only given for the Sabbath when medical procedures are forbidden if there is no danger to the life of the patient.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Idi, Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: with hand or vessel<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">“Hand” means to pour an amont of oil into one’s palm and taking it from there by a finger of the other hand. “Vessel” is not the large container of the oil but a small vessel containing just enough oil for the procedure. Its use is permitted on the Sabbath for people who would be repelled by the idea of dipping the finger into the jar of cooking oil.</i>. In what do they differ? Whether it is repulsive. For him who says with hand or vessel, it is repulsive. For him who says neither hand nor vessel, it is not repulsive. Rebbi Jonah said, they differ about Second Tithe. For him who says with hand or vessel, Second Tithe is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden to use Second Tithe oil for medical purposes; it is permitted to use it for anointing. It is suggested that just smearing oil on one’s head with a finger is a form of anointing; this is rejected immediately.</i>. For him who says neither hand nor vessel, Second Tithe is permitted. Rebbi Yose said, is all that is permitted on the Sabbath permitted with Second Tithe and all that is forbidden on the Sabbath forbidden with Second Tithe? Did we not state: A woman may douse her son with wine<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">On the Sabbath. In <i>Šabbat</i> 14:3, the text reads: “Herself and her son.”</i> because of sweat, but this is forbidden with heave? Heave and Second Tithe are the same! How is that<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">What is the real reason for the rule of whispering? Anybody who does it with a vessel on weekdays has to do it without one on the Sabbath and vice-versa.</i>? Only that he not do it in the way he is used to do it on weekdays.
<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is from <i>Terumot</i> 10:1; explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 2:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.2.1.2">Notes 8–12</a>.</i>“If fish is cooked with Second Tithe leeks which increases its value, the increase is proportional.” Rebbi Hoshaia said, it follows Rebbi Jehudah, since we have stated: “Rebbi Jehudah permits in anchovies where it only serves to remove pollutants.” The rabbis of Caesarea asked: The saying of Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, that all forbidden [food] is estimated as if it were onion, as if it were leeks, does this contradict Rebbi Jehudah? Rebbi Jehudah will agree regarding an onion of the Temple or an onion of idol worship.
<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the last part of the Mishnah.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, for everything that augments the measure, the increase [in value] is proportional, but for everything that does not augment the measure, the increase belongs to the Second [Tithe]. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, for everything where the taste of the improvement is recognizable, the increase [in value] is proportional, but for everything where the taste of the improvement is not recognizable, the increase belongs to the Second [Tithe]. The Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Jonathan<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the opinion of R. Simeon ben Laqish, which reaffirms the statement of the Mishnah, is that of R. Jonathan.</i>: “If a dough of Second Tithe when baked increased in value, the increase belongs to the Second.” Explain it if the improvement cannot be tasted. The Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: “If fish cooked with Second Tithe leeks increased in value, the increase is proportional.” Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: Explain it if he cooked them together<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">He cooked them together until they became soft and could no longer be separated. Since the profane part clinging to the heave part must be eaten in the holiness of heave, the volume of the holy part has increased.</i>. Rebbi Jonah in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: Think of it, if he cooked them separately and mixed them<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, there would only be a question according to R. Simeon ben Laqish but none according to R. Joḥanan.</i>! Have fish only the taste of leeks and leeks only the taste of fish<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Since in the latter case only little taste is absorbed, both R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish agree that the increase of the value of the tithe part does not have to be split.</i>?
A Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: “Spices, water, or salt a woman borrowed from a friend for her dough, goes after both their feet<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 5:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.5.4.1">Mishnah <i>Beẓah</i> 5:4</a>. If one or both of the women had moved their domain for the holiday by an <i>eruv</i> (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 8:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.8.4.4"><i>Peah</i> 8, Note 56</a>), during the holiday the dough cannot be brought to any place not reachable by both of them. This part of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 5:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.5.3.1">Mishnah 5:4</a> is not discussed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 5:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.5.5.2">Yerushalmi <i>Beẓah</i> 5:4</a>.<br>The Yerushalmi is difficult to understand. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 38b-39a" href="/Beitzah.38b-39a">Babli (<i>Beẓah</i> 38b–39a</a>) comes up with three possible explanations for the Mishnah. Abbai holds that all is only a rabbinical prohibition to be ready with a rule for the case the two women baked a cake together. Rava holds with R. Simeon ben Laqish that taste cannot be disregarded and, therefore, the spices are essential even if present only in a minute amount. As Tosaphot point out, water certainly cannot be disregarded even if most of it evaporates since without water there would be no dough. But water used to make unleavened dough does not necessarily increase the volume. This seems to be the objection of the Yerushalmi. Rav Ashi holds that one does not invoke special rules if by waiting (ubtil the end of the holiday) all problems disappear automatically.</i>.” Rebbi Abba said, for <i>eruv</i> they went according to the logical rule<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Since the dish contains contributions from both of them, one does not have to try to allocate parts.</i>. You should know that this is so since they say there<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia. In this version, the dish can be moved only in the common domain even if only the firewood came from a person with a different domain. Firewood cannot be bought with tithe money.</i> in the name of Rav Ḥisda, but we know not whether from a tradition or a Mishnah, “even logs!” Did we want to say that logs have no credible importance? A <i>baraita</i><sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Maaser Sheni (Lieberman) 1:16" href="/Tosefta_Maaser_Sheni_(Lieberman).1.16">Tosephta 1:16</a>.</i> disagrees with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: “If a tithe dish is prepared with profane spices, the increase belongs to Second Tithe.” Explain it if the improvement cannot be tasted. But was it not stated: “If a profane dish is prepared with Second Tithe spices, the tithe cannot escape redemption<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Or the entire dish must be eaten under the rules of tithe. This part of the Tosephta requires explanation according to both opinions.</i>”? According to Rebbi Joḥanan, if the volume was increased. According to Rebbi Simeon ber Laqish if the improvement was clearly recognizable.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Simeon says, one does not rub himself with oil of Second Tithe in Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">In the opinion of R. Simson and R. Isaac Simponti, R. Simeon disagrees with the previous Mishnah and Halakhah and holds that anointing is not subsumed under the notion of eating. This is contradicted by the Halakhah. In the opinion of Maimonides, R. Simeon agrees that in principle rubbing oneself is legitimate use of Second Tithe oil. However, he holds the owner of Second Tithe oil may not ask another person to massage him because the latter’s hand also will be rubbed with oil and this is akin to paying for the massage with Second Tithe oil, a rabbinic transgression. The last assertion is difficult to accept; the more likely explanation is that of H. Albeck (<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup>ששה סדרי משנה, Jerusalem 1958), that according to R. Simeon if a person rubbed with such oil leaves Jerusalem while some of the oil still clings to his body, he will have sinned. In order to prevent inadvertent sin he forbids rubbing as a “fence of the law.”</i> but the Sages permit it. They said to Rebbi Simeon, if He made it easy for the important heave<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the next Mishnah.</i>, should we not be lenient for the lightweight Second Tithe? He said to them, this is no good<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading of the Yerushalmi mss., the Parma ms. of the Mishnah, and one ms. of a translation of the Commentary of Maimonides. All other Mishnah mss. read מה לא אם היקל “On the contrary, if He was lenient …”</i>. He was lenient for the important heave as He was lenient for vetch and fenugreek<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.2.2.1">Mishnah 4</a>. If the argument is accepted, the relation of heave to Second Tithe is not that of <i>majus</i> to <i>minor</i>.</i>; how can we be lenient for Second Tithe where He was not lenient for vetch and fenugreek?<br>Fenugreek of Second Tithe should be eaten as sprouts<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Since Second Tithe must be eaten; ripe fenugreek plants are hard as straw and its seeds also are hard; the only time fenugreek really is edible is in young sprouts.</i>. Of Heave<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned several times in <i>Terumot</i> and <i>Ma‘serot</i>, heave of fenugreek is traded in bundles, the hard stem (which gives some taste) and the seeds (usable as spice). The House of Shammai hold that since it may be used as spice, it must always be treated as food and handled in purity since otherwise it must be burned as impure heave food, except that it can be used to perfume water to shampoo hair. In that case, the Cohen’s action shows that the fenugreek is not food for him and if impure does not have to be burned.</i>, the House of Shammai say it must always be handled in purity except for shampooing. But the House of Hillel say, it may be handled in impurity except in soaking<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">For the House of Hillel, fenugreek is not food except if shown by an action of the Cohen to be prepared as food, e. g., soaking the seeds to use as spice. Otherwise, fenugreek is wood and cannot become impure; if it is handled in impurity it does not have to be burned.</i>.<br>Vetch<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">It was stated (<i>Terumot</i> 11:9) that only in years of famine is vetch human food. But as sprouts it can be eaten and is not animal feed. This Mishnah also appears in <i>Idiut</i> 1:8.</i> of Second Tithe should be eaten as sprouts; it may enter Jerusalem and leave<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Since sprouts cannot qualify as produce.</i>. If it became impure, Rebbi Ṭarphon says it should be divided into pieces of dough<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">He does not permit to redeem any Second Tithe to be used as animal feed; therefore he requires the vetch, which is a legume, to be ground to flour and mixed with flour in little cakes whose volume is less than that of a chicken egg so that it may be eaten as pure human food (cf. <i>Terumot</i> 5:3).</i>, but the Sages say it should be redeemed<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">And fed to animals after redemption.</i>. If it is heave<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Heave vetch is animal feed, <i>Terumot</i> 11:9.</i>, the House of Shammai say, one soaks and cleans<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the intention was from the start to use it as animal feed, as long as it is not given to the animals it must strictly be treated according to the rules of heave.</i> in purity and feeds in impurity, but the House of Hillel say, one soaks in purity<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">In order not to make heave impure with one’s hands.</i> and cleans and feeds in impurity. Shammai says, it should be eaten dry<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">It should be given dry and unwashed to the animals to avoid any question of impurity.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba says, all its processing is done in impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that vetch is not human food unless there is a famine.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> How were they lenient for heave? As we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 10:10" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).10.10">Tosephta <i>Terumot</i> 10:10</a>. The Babli, based on biblical verses, explains in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 7a" href="/Keritot.7a"><i>Keritut</i> 7a</a> that rubbing oil on one’s body, while a permitted activity, removes the sanctity from the oil just as eating removes the sanctity from the food in the Cohen’s body. Therefore, even if an unrelated adult Israel touches the oil on the Cohen’s body, he does not sin.</i>: “A Cohen may rub himself with heave oil and bring his daughter’s son by an Israel and roll him on his belly without qualms.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, here they retorted with rabbinic words against the word of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Simeon’s prohibition is rabbinical, the argument of the Sages is biblical!<br>The Rome ms. reads דברי סופרים לדברי סופרים. This seems to be a scribal error.</i>.
So is the Mishnah: It is permitted to eat it as sprouts<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">It is not a requirement. J. N. Epstein (487 מבוא לנוסח המשנה ע׳) considers this to be a commentary, not a change of the Mishnah text.</i>.
What is between them? Rebbi Jonah said, pulling out of water is between them. The House of Shammai say, one pulls out of the water with pure hands but the House of Hillel say, one pulls out of the water with impure hands<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">In this version, the House of Hillel require pure (i. e., washed) hands only for putting fenugreek into the water for soaking, not to make heave impure by one’s own action. But they will permit taking the soaked seeds out of the water with unwashed hands since the seeds are also soaked as animal feed.</i>. It is stated<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Maaser Sheni (Lieberman) 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Maaser_Sheni_(Lieberman).2.1">Tosephta 2:1</a>.</i>: “These are the words of Rebbi Meïr. But the words of Rebbi Jehudah, he says that the House of Shammai say, it must always be handled in purity except for shampooing. But the House of Hillel say, it may be handled in impurity except in pulling it out of water<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The text is uncertain. The Tosephta has שרייתה “it soaking” instead of שליתה. It is possible that שליה here does not mean “to scoop out of the water”, as in Yerushalmi Targumim but by a change of liquids <i>l</i> and <i>r</i> is the same as שריה, “soaking”, and refers to all stages of soaking. This is not the position of Maimonides in his Code (<i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i> 2:14).</i>.” What is between them? Rebbi Mattaniah said, softening<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Softening the kernels by rubbing them between one’s fingers. The House of Hillel do not consider this an action transforming the seeds into food.</i> is between them. The House of Shammai say, one softens with pure hands but the House of Hillel say, one softens with impure hands.
To make a dough and return it, to make it and return<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">This seems to refer to the statement that Second Tithe vetch may leave Jerusalem. An identical statement is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 3:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.3.3.3">Halakhah 3:4</a>, referring to the opinion of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.3.3.1">Mishnah 3:5</a>) that Second Tithe produce which entered Jerusalem may leave again. It is stated here and there that it may leave only to be processed, returned, and consumed in Jerusalem.<br>The duplication in the text is difficult to understand; instead of כדי לעשות ולחזור the Rome ms. reads כדי בגיסה ולחזור. The meaning of the <i>hapax legomenon</i> בגיסה (assuming it is not a corruption) is unknown. The text in 3:4 has a simple duplication כדי לעשות עיסה ולחזור. כדי לעשות עיסה ולחזור.</i>.
Is our Mishnah Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel’s<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">This seems to refer to the statement that Second Tithe vetch may leave Jerusalem. An identical statement is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 3:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.3.3.3">Halakhah 3:4</a>, referring to the opinion of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.3.3.1">Mishnah 3:5</a>) that Second Tithe produce which entered Jerusalem may leave again. It is stated here and there that it may leave only to be processed, returned, and consumed in Jerusalem.<br>The duplication in the text is difficult to understand; instead of כדי לעשות ולחזור the Rome ms. reads כדי בגיסה ולחזור. The meaning of the <i>hapax legomenon</i> בגיסה (assuming it is not a corruption) is unknown. The text in 3:4 has a simple duplication כדי לעשות עיסה ולחזור. כדי לעשות עיסה ולחזור.</i>? “Also produce enters and leaves”! It is the opinion of everybody. Here they were very lenient for vetch, but only dough of vetch<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">For the Halakhah here, dough of Second Tithe produce for <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 3:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.3.3.3">Halakhah 3:4</a>.</i>, only dough of Second Tithe.
Rebbi Gorion in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: Rebbi Ṭarphon implies that one may not redeem sanctified food to feed it to the dogs. Rebbi Yose said<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">He gives the rationale of the Sages opposing R. Tarphon.</i>, it seems reasonable that anything which can be human food cannot be redeemed to be fed to animals, but anything which is not human food can be redeemed to be fed to animals.
Rebbi Isaac ben Eliashiv asked: [Is it true that] if it became impure in the countryside it should not be redeemed since one may not redeem sanctified food to feed it to the dogs? Rebbi Jonah said, that is <sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The Rome ms. reads ניטומטומי. All commentators read the expression as one of concurrence with the rule tentatively expressed by R. Isaac ben Eliashiv. The expression has not been convincingly explained. {The Latin and Greek etymologies offered by Levy, Löw (in Krauss’s Dictionary), Kohout, and Lieberman (<i>Tarbiẓ</i> 3, p. 337) presuppose rewriting the consonantal text. Jastrow declares the text corrupt.}</i>נוטי נוטומי.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Of profane and Second Tithe coins that were strewn around, what one collected goes to Second Tithe until complete, the remainder is profane<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">One assumes that the profane and the tithe coins are indistinguishable. What is collected first has to be sanctified as Second Tithe money by a declaration, and what had been Second Tithe coins and now ends up in the profane part is then automatically redeemed by the profane coins which ended up in the tithe part.</i>. If he mixed them together and took out fists full, it is in proportion<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">If the coins are not picked up as they fell but first are swept together with a broom to form a heap in which all coins are mixed, the coins taken out are separated into heave and profane according to the proportion heave and profane had before the accident. According to Maimonides (in his Commentary and his Code, <i>Ma‘aśer Šeni</i> 6:1) the proportional split in this case holds even if some coins were lost. In this case also, a declaration of substitution has to be made.</i>. That is the principle: What is picked out is for Second Tithe and what is mixed together by proportion<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">For all Second Tithe, not only coins. This is spelled out more clearly in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Maaser Sheni (Lieberman) 2:4" href="/Tosefta_Maaser_Sheni_(Lieberman).2.4">Tosephta, 2:4</a>.</i>.<br>If a tetradrachma of Second Tithe was mixed up with a profane one<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">And it is not known which of the two coins is dedicated as Second Tithe. The farmer does not want to take both coins to Jerusalem.</i>, one brings coins in the value of a tetradrachma and says: The tetradrachma of Second Tithe, wherever it may be, is exchanged for these coins. Then he chooses the better of the two and exchanges it for them since they said, one exchanges bronze for silver in an emergency<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">But not silver for silver. When the silver coin, the standard legal tender in the entire Roman empire, is spent in Jerusalem it is usually first converted into local bronze coin. Therefore, this exchange is legal under certain circumstances. But silver coins under normal circumstances are never exchanged into other silver coins and, therefore, such an exchange is never permitted. The bronze coins should not be kept as such because non-local bronze coins may not be accepted at full value in Jerusalem.</i>, not that it should stay so but that he should exchange them for silver.<br>The House of Shammai say, a person should not turn his tetradrachmas into gold denars but the House of Hillel permit it. Rebbi Aqiba said, for Rabban Gamliel and Rebbi Joshua I turned their silver coins into gold denars.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Zeïra said, for the profit of the Second; maybe the rest will be lost and then at least those held in his hand will be kept for Second [Tithe]<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">He explains why the person who does not first sweep all coins together must first collect for Second Tithe. The person who sweeps (and in the opinion of R. Jonah, the person who collects in systematic sweeps) does something first to avoid further losses and, therefore, can profit from more lenient rules.</i>. Rebbi Zeïra said, and one has to spell out and say, if those at the bottom are from the Second, those in my hand are taken in their stead. Rebbi Jonah said, only if he collected from all around but if he collected in a straight line it is as if he had swept and taken out fists full.
<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">The first three sentences are also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 5:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.5.5"><i>Demay</i> 5:5. Notes 82–83</a>. The discussion is about the principle, applied to produce, stated at the end of the Mishnah.</i> Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Pedaiah, Rebbi Jonah in the name of Ḥizqiah, there is no mixing except for wine and oil. Rebbi Joḥanan says, it can be mixed up to the size of olives<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Solid produce smaller than the size of an olive, e. g., grains, can be thoroughly mixed.</i>. Our Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: “If he mixed them together and took out fists full, it is by proportion.” He explains it, up to the size of olives. “What is picked out is for Second Tithe and what is mixed together in proportion.” Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Huna: The Mishnah comes up so: What is mixed and<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">One should not read “mixed <i>or</i> taken out in fistfuls” but “mixed <i>and</i> taken out in fistfuls”.</i> what is taken out in fists full in proprtion.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Maaser Sheni (Lieberman) 2:5" href="/Tosefta_Maaser_Sheni_(Lieberman).2.5">Tosephta 2:5</a>. Ben Azai requires small change in double the value of the tithe coin.</i>: Ben Azai says, two. Rebbi Zeïra said, in a dream I had Ulla bar Ismael ate fat meat<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">R. Zeïra, the dreamer, took that to mean that Ulla was fed celestial fare which contains the secrets of the Torah.</i>. The next day he came and asked him, he said to him, why two? He said to him, since you tell him so, he will push himself to exchange it<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">If one exchanges for the exact value, the farmer will not see why he could not simply take one of the coins and declare it tithe money. But then the original tithe coin might be spent in a sinful way as profane.</i>.
Ḥizqiah said, when he exchanges it, he treats it as bad. But if he exchanges for it, he treats it as a good one<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">If one exchanges for the exact value, the farmer will not see why he could not simply take one of the coins and declare it tithe money. But then the original tithe coin might be spent in a sinful way as profane.</i>. But did we not state: “He chooses the better of the two and exchanges it for them<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah seems to establish higher, not lower, standards for transactions involving his own Second Tithe.</i>”? Why can he not take the worse [coin] and treat it as a good one? Rebbi Jonah said, I say that this one was Second Tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">The trick of Ḥizqiah is acceptable if a profane coin is turned into tithe money. But the moment it is dedicated as tithe, it has to be treated with the respect due the holy.</i>.
Did it not become profane anyhow<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Why does one have to insist that the bronze coins be changed into the present silver coin in the end? Why can the two silver coins not become totally profane?</i>? Rebbi Jonah said, he should not intend to make it totally profane. You should understand that this is so since we have stated: “not that it should stay so but that he should exchange it for silver”.
Rebbi Ḥaggai said before Rebbi Zeïra, Menaḥem in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: In all cases where they said about <i>demay</i> that it can be exchanged<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in Mishnah <i>Demay</i>1:2 that for tithe money of <i>demay</i> one may exchange silver for silver, silver for bronze, bronze for bronze, etc.</i>, for certain produce one may exchange it in an emergency<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Not only the one case stated in the Mishnah here.</i>. But did we not state: “One exchanges bronze for silver in an emergency”, therefore not silver for silver! Rebbi Abba bar Cohen said before Rebbi Jonah, Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: In all cases where they said about <i>demay</i> that it should be exchanged, if he transgressed and exchanged for certain produce it is exchanged<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">While it is illegal, the exchange is valid. Only the case stated by the Mishnah is legal. This corrects the tradition reported by R. Ḥaggai. The difference between R. Abba bar Cohen and R. Jehudah ben Pazi is a minor point of grammar.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi, Rebbi Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: In all cases where they said about <i>demay</i> that it should be exchanged, if he transgressed and exchanged for certain produce they are exchanged.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Maaser Sheni (Lieberman) 2:7" href="/Tosefta_Maaser_Sheni_(Lieberman).2.7">Tosephta 2:7</a>: “Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon says one <i>should be able to exchange</i> gold for silver just as one exchanges silver for bronze. Rebbi said to him: Why does one exchange silver for bronze? Because one exchanges silver for gold! <i>Then one should not be able to</i> exchange gold for silver <i>since</i> one does not exchange gold for bronze! Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon answered him: Certainly Second Tithe gold one exchanges for small coins in Jerusalem! <i>When has this been said? For</i> demay <i>but not for certain tithe</i>!” Since the Yerushalmi does not consider <i>demay</i> in this connection, the Tosephta cannot be the basis of the Yerushalmi.</i>: “Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon says one exchanges gold for silver just as one exchanges silver for bronze<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Since this exchange (outside of Jerusalem) is authorized only in emergencies, the exchange of gold for silver also should be authorized only in emergencies.</i>. Rebbi said to him: Why does one exchange silver for bronze? Because one exchanges silver for gold<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">The opinion of the House of Hillel in the next Mishnah.</i>! Should one exchange gold for silver<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">The standard means of payment was the silver tetradrachma. Gold coins have to be exchanged by a competent money changer and bronze/copper coins are only local currency. In both cases one reduces the immediate availability of the money in Jerusalem and this should be avoided.</i>? One does not exchange gold for bronze! Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon answered him: Certainly one exchanges Second Tithe gold for small coins in Jerusalem!” From the arguments of both of them one exchanges silver for silver and one does not exchange silver for bronze<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">While this is the text in both mss. and the <i>editio princeps</i>, it is clear that one should read: “one exchanges silver for bronze and one does not exchange silver for silver.” An exchange of silver for silver would not accomplish anything.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, the House of Shammai said this only at the end, but at the beginning also the House of Shammai agree<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">In this interpretation, the House of Shammai require that any coin of tithe money taken to Jerusalem should be a silver coin since it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:24-25" href="/Deuteronomy.14.24-25">Deut. 14:24–25</a>): “If the way is too much for you that you cannot carry it all the way … then <i>turn it into silver, take the silver in your hand</i> and go to the place which the Eternal, your God, will choose.”<br>An echo of the discussion here is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 45a" href="/Bava_Metzia.45a">Babli <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 45a</a>. In the final interpretation there, the reason of the House of Shammai is rabbinical, not biblical.</i>. What is the reason of the House of Shammai? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:25" href="/Deuteronomy.14.25"><i>Deut</i>. 14:25</a>) “Silver”, not gold. Say “silver” but not bronze! But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.2.4.1">Mishnah 2:8</a>, seemingly about exchanging large coins for small change outside of Jerusalem. The interpretation turns the language of the Mishnah on its head and reads: “He who takes small coins of Second Tithe money and turns them into large coins,” which is permitted. This interpretation is accepted by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Bekhorot 50b" href="/Rashi_on_Bekhorot.50b">Rashi (<i>Bekhorot</i> 50b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 11b" href="/Kiddushin.11b"><i>Qiddušin</i> 11b</a>) since nobody starts out exchanging produce for silver coins.</i>: “If somebody gets change for coins of Second Tithe”? We wanted to say that the Mishnah means: If somebody takes<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">מצרף is the same as the corresponding Arabic صرف “to exchange money” in the sense of giving large coins for small, in contrast to פורט, giving change for large coins. (J. N. Epstein, מבוא לנוסח המשנה<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup>, p. 487.)</i> a tetradrachma for coins of Second Tithe. How is that? The reason of the House of Shammai is that silver and bronze have the same rule<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">It is unreasonable to expect the Second Tithe of one year to amount to a tetradrachma’s worth. Therefore, they must permit the original redemption in copper coins to be turned into silver but exclude gold coins.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, both at the beginning and at the end does the House of Shammai disagree. What is the reason of the House of Shammai? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:25" href="/Deuteronomy.14.25"><i>Deut</i>. 14:25</a>) “Silver” first and not second<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">We think that he takes the verse “then <i>turn it into silver</i>” to mean that redemption of Second Tithe is possible only with silver coin.</i>. But did we not state: “If somebody gets change for coins of Second Tithe”? We wanted to say that the Mishnah means: If somebody takes a tetradrachma for coins of Second Tithe. How is that? The reason of the House of Shammai (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:25" href="/Deuteronomy.14.25"><i>Deut</i>. 14:25</a>): “Silver”, it should add up to silver<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">He really only insists on the second part of the verse, <i>take the silver in your hand</i>, that one should not go to Jerusalem unless he has at least a silver coin of Second Tithe money to spend there. But even if one has 25 silver coins to carry there, the House of Shammai will not permit them to be changed into a gold denar. The House of Hillel interpret כסף as “money” in all these verses.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who gives a tetradrachma for change of Second Tithe money<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 2:3:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.2.3.9">Note 109</a>. In this interpretation, the House of Hillel require bronze coins to be exchanged into silver coins at the earliest possible moment since bronze coins are more likely to deteriorate.</i>, the House of Shammai say small change for the entire tetradrachma but the House of Hillel say one <i>šeqel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Two drachmas (silver denars), half a tetradrachma. This change is outside of Jerusalem. As R. Abraham ben David explains in his commentary to the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Eduyot 1:9" href="/Mishnah_Eduyot.1.9">Mishnah (<i>Idiut</i> 1:9</a>), the House of Hillel think that if everybody brings only silver coin to Jerusalem, the money changers there will raise the price of bronze coins relative to silver.</i> silver coin and one <i>šeqel</i> small coin. Rebbi Meïr says, one does not exchange silver coin and produce for silver coin<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody has three silver denars and produce in the value of one denar of Second Tithe, he should not exchange them together for a tetradrachma.</i>, but the Sages permit it.<br>He who gives a tetradrachma for change of Second Tithe money<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 2:3:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.2.3.9">Note 109</a>. In this interpretation, the House of Hillel require bronze coins to be exchanged into silver coins at the earliest possible moment since bronze coins are more likely to deteriorate.</i>, the House of Shammai say small change for the entire tetradrachma but the House of Hillel say one <i>šeqel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Two drachmas (silver denars), half a tetradrachma. This change is outside of Jerusalem. As R. Abraham ben David explains in his commentary to the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Eduyot 1:9" href="/Mishnah_Eduyot.1.9">Mishnah (<i>Idiut</i> 1:9</a>), the House of Hillel think that if everybody brings only silver coin to Jerusalem, the money changers there will raise the price of bronze coins relative to silver.</i> silver coin and one <i>šeqel</i> small coin. Rebbi Meïr says, one does not exchange silver coin and produce for silver coin<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody has three silver denars and produce in the value of one denar of Second Tithe, he should not exchange them together for a tetradrachma.</i>, but the Sages permit it.<br>If part of one’s children were pure and part impure<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">All are forbidden Second Tithe.</i>, he puts down the tetradrachma<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Of tithe money.</i> and says, this tetradrachma shall be exchanged for what the pure are drinking. It turns out that the pure and the impure may drink from the same pitcher<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">The wine must be pure so that it can become Second Tithe the moment it is poured into the cups of the pure children.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, where do Rebbi Meïr and the rabbis disagree? If the produce is not worth a silver coin. But for produce worth a silver coin also the rabbis agree. Half a denar in money and half a denar’s worth of produce is permitted. A denar in money and a denar’s worth of produce is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Since silver coin should not be exchanged for silver coin; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 2:3:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.2.3.8">Note 107</a>.</i>, and so much more two denars in money and two denar’s worth of produce is forbidden.
These are the ones who argue: Ben Azai and Ben Zoma. These are the students: Ḥanina ben Haḵiniah and Rebbi Eleazar ben Mathia<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 17b" href="/Sanhedrin.17b">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 17b</a>, those who argue before the Sages are Simeon ben Azai, Simeon ben Zoma, Ḥanan the Egyptian, Ḥanina ben Hakinai, and possibly Simeon the Yemenite. According to Rashi, they argue before the Sages, or are called students, because they all died young before they could establish their own schools.</i>. The holy group, Rebbi Yose ben Hameshullam and Rebbi Simeon ben Menasiah<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">They are called holy because they studied all the time they could but worked to sustain their families, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kohelet Rabbah 9:9" href="/Kohelet_Rabbah.9.9"><i>Qohelet rabba</i> on 9:9</a>.</i>.
What is the reason of Shammai? Maybe he will forget and treat it as profane<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">If he has both profane and tithe moneys, he will not be able to keep them separate and might spend tithe money on unauthorized expenditures.<br>The Rome ms. reads וישתה for ויעשׂה; which seems to be a scribal error.</i>!
Where do we hold? If he<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">The father mentioned in Mishnah 10. He cannot declare part of the wine as Second Tithe before it is poured since then the impure children will also drink from Second Tithe. He cannot declare anything after the wine was consumed. He must declare beforehand that his declaration is conditional and applies only when the wine is poured and drunk.</i> said it before, the drink is mixed. If he said it when it will be drunk, before they drank profane. But we hold that he said it before to apply at the time of drinking.
If they are impure by the impurity of the dead, for no clay vessel becomes impure from its back<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">What kind of impurity do the impure children have? A clay vessel can make its contents impure only if it became impure inside its cavity, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:33" href="/Leviticus.11.33"><i>Lev</i>. 11:33</a>. In this case, even an impure child may handle the pitcher and pour pure wine for his pure siblings.</i>. But if they are impure by the impurity of flux<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Somebody impure because of a genital discharge causes impurity to anything he carries, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:10" href="/Leviticus.15.10"><i>Lev</i>. 15:10</a>. In this case, the impure person may not move the pitcher.</i>, since the impure from flux makes impure by carrying, this cannot be the case unless one [other person] pours. But if one [other] pours, even if they are impure by the impurity of flux.

Chapter 3



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A person should not say to another: bring this produce to Jerusalem to distribute<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">He cannot promise the other person a piece of the Second Tithe as reward for his transporting the tithe since that would be a commercial transaction forbidden by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.1.1.1">Mishnah 1:1</a>. He can either invite the other to join in the consumption of the tithe or he can give it all to the other as an unconditional gift; the other person will take it up to Jerusalem, and then may give it to the original owner as an unconditional gift.</i>, but he may say bring it that we shall eat and drink it together, or they may give free gifts to one another.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph and the next appear (with a natural change in the order of the first two subjects) in <i>Ševi‘it</i> 8:4, and are explained there, Notes 51–62.</i>“One should not say to another person,” etc. What is the difference between him who says “to distribute” and him who says “that we should eat and drink them?” Rebbi Zeïra in the name of Rebbi Jonathan, this is one of the practices of obfuscation. There, we have stated: If somebody says to a day-laborer: “Here you have an <i>as</i> and collect vegetables for me today”, these wages are permitted. “For its value collect vegetables for me today”, these wages are forbidden. If somebody bought from the baker a loaf in the value of a <i>dupondius</i> [and says], when I collect vegetables from the field I shall bring to you, that is permitted. If he took from him silently he should not pay with the proceeds of Sabbatical [produce] since one may not pay a debt with proceeds of the Sabbatical. What is the difference between him who says “collect for me” and him who says “for its value collect for me”? Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina, this is one of the practices of obfuscation. There, we have stated: “A person may borrow from another pitchers of wine or oil but he should not say: lend me.” What is the difference between him who says “let me borrow” and him who says “lend me”? Rebbi Zeïra in the name of Rebbi Jonathan, this is one of the practices of obfuscation.
Their bread. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: This also belongs to the practices of obfuscation. Rebbi Yose said, I objected before Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa: Why should it belong to the practices of obfuscation? Do I say that at a place where Jewish bread is available, Gentile bread should be forbidden but they obfuscated about it and permitted it or that at a place where no Jewish bread is available it is logical that Gentile bread should be permitted, but they obfuscated the matter and forbade it. Rebbi Mana said: Does there exist obfuscation for prohibition? Is bread not like Gentile cooking? So we say: At a place where no Jewish cooking is to be found it is logical that Gentile cooking should be permitted, but they obfuscated the matter and forbade it. But so it was: At a place where no Jewish bread is available it is logical that Gentile bread should be forbidden, but they obfuscated the matter and permitted it as a necessity of life. The rabbis of Caesarea in the name of Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa [hold] with the one who permits, but only from a store. But we do not follow this.
<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This also belongs to <i>Ševi‘it</i> 8:4; it is there in rudimentary form, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 8:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.8.4.5"><i>Ševi‘it</i>8, Note 69</a>.</i> It was stated: “Rebbi Jehudah and Rebbi Neḥemiah forbid it.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sheviit (Lieberman) 6:21" href="/Tosefta_Sheviit_(Lieberman).6.21">Tosephta <i>Ševi‘it</i> 6:21</a>: “If somebody buys from the baker a loaf for a <i>dupondius</i> [and says] I shall collect vegetables from the field this is permitted [in the Sabbatical]. Rebbis Jehudah and Neḥemiah forbid it.”</i>” What are we talking about? If about him<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The baker. In this formulation, Sabbatical vegetables would be used for the payment of a debt; this is forbidden.</i> who says, here you have it, give me [payment], everybody agrees that it is forbidden. If he<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The buyer.</i> says, give me and I shall give you, everybody agrees that it is permitted. But it is about one who says, give it to me and it is clear to me [where to find it], I shall bring it to you; Rebbi Jehudah and Rebbi Neḥemiah forbid it because vegetables on the field are rare; but the Sages permit it because vegetables on the field are abundant.
After he transported it, may he say take your part and I shall take mine<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">If the tithe is already in Jerusalem, is the language which was barred in the Mishnah acceptable?</i>? May he say to him, take this barrel of wine<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">And transport it to Jerusalem.</i> and we shall eat the barrel of oil that is there. Is it similar with animal tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This question refers both to the situation described in the Mishnah and the two questions raised here.</i>? Second Tithe, since it has special conditions for a sale<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">While it is forbidden to sell Second Tithe, it may be exchanged for money and then sold.</i> you say is forbidden but animal tithe since it has no conditions for a sale<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Redemption and any use of animal tithe other than as sacrifice or food of the family of the original rancher is strictly forbidden; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:32-33" href="/Leviticus.27.32-33"><i>Lev</i>. 27:32–33</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.1.1.1">Mishnah 1:2</a>.</i> you say is permitted. It should not be clear to you that it is permitted. May he ask him, take and transport this domesticated or wild animal, and we shall eat meat from a ritually slaughtered animal there<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to make sense of the question in the framework of tithes. The best explanation seems to be that of the author of <i>Sepher Nir</i> who emends בהמה וחיה into בהמה חיה and reads: “May he ask another person, take and transport this live animal [of tithe], and we shall eat [its] meat [once it is] ritually slaughtered [in the Temple] there?” It is possible to call meat from the sacrifice of animal tithe “slaughtered meat”, an expression usually reserved for profane meat, since only the blood of the animal is sprinkled on the altar but no part of it is burned or given to a Cohen.</i>?

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One may not buy heave with tithe money because he would restrict its edibility<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">To Cohanim as explained in the Halakhah.</i>, but Rebbi Simeon permits. Rebbi Simeon said to them: If He was lenient with well-being sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">As established in Chapter 1, that meat bought with tithe money should be meat from well-being sacrifices.</i> when that could bring them to be <i>piggul</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">A sacrifice brought with the intent of eating it at an inappropriate time or inappropriate place, a deadly sin (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:18" href="/Leviticus.7.18"><i>Lev</i>. 7:18</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:7" href="/Leviticus.19.7">19:7</a>).</i>, leftover<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Leftovers from sacrificial meat remaining after the allotted time, whose consumption is sinful.</i>, or impure<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The rules of impurity for sacrifices are much more restrictive than those for heave; in turn, the rules of impurity for heave are much more restrictive than those for Second Tithe. However, in the opinion of most tannaïtic authorities, these rules are purely rabbinical. Therefore, it is possible that “or impure” is an intrusion from the standard list of disqualifications of a sacrifice, פיגול נותר וטמא.</i>, would He not be lenient with heave? They answered him but no, if He was lenient with well-being sacrifices that are permitted to outsiders<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Non-Cohanim.</i>, can we be lenient with heave which is forbidden to outsiders?
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: “That it should not become unusable<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Heave from Second Tithe can become unusable in a way Second Tithe could not.</i>.” What is “become unusable”? Rebbi Jonah said, it becomes unusable through a <i>ṭevul-yom</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">A previously impure person in the time between his immersion in a ritual bath and sundown; if he touches heave or sacrifices they become unusable, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 5:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.5.2.2"><i>Terumot</i> Chapter 5, Note 68</a>.</i>. You cannot eat it, for he is impure by the word of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">He really is not impure, as stated in the next clause. But since the verse says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:7" href="/Leviticus.22.7"><i>Lev</i>. 22:7</a>): “at sundown he will be pure”, in regard to heave and sacrifices he has a biblical status of not being pure.</i>; you cannot for he is pure by the word of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The requirement of waiting till sundown is spelled out only in connection with heave and sacrifices. In all other circumstances of impurity mentioned in <i>Lev</i>., there is only a requirement of immersion in water.</i>. That means, that it should not become unusable<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">It will be stated in Mishnah 10 that impure Second Tithe in Jerusalem can be redeemed and eaten as impure food. But heave, suspended between purity and impurity, does not qualify for redemption or any other use.</i>.
What means “he would restrict its edibility”? Heave is forbidden to outsiders, Second Tithe is permitted to outsiders. Heave is forbidden to a <i>ṭevul-yom</i>, Second Tithe is permitted to a <i>ṭevul-yom</i>. And just as he would restrict its edibility, so he would restrict her<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">“She” is heave; to make the point understandable the feminine has to be used also in the English translation.</i> edibility. Heave is permitted to the deep mourner, Second Tithe is forbidden to the deep mourner<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The “deep mourner” is the close relative of a deceased person in the period between death and burial. While the prohibition is not spelled out, it is implied by the text of the declaration to be made at the presentation of Second Tithe in the Temple (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:14" href="/Deuteronomy.26.14"><i>Deut</i>. 26:14</a>): “I did not eat from it in my deep mourning.”</i>. Heave does not need an enclosure, Second Tithe needs an enclosure<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Second Tithe must be eaten in the holy precinct; the expression “enclosure” is broad enough to cover the holy precinct of Shiloh and the walls of Jerusalem. {It is questionable whether the extant Turkish walls of Jerusalem would quality, cf. Mishnah <i>Ševu‘ot</i> 2:2.}</i>. It was found stated: “Because he would restrict its edibility and her edibility”.
<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The paragraph is also Halakhah 9:7 in <i>Pesaḥim</i>, fol. 37a.</i> It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">A similar statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sheviit (Lieberman) 7:1" href="/Tosefta_Sheviit_(Lieberman).7.1">Tosephta <i>Ševi‘it</i> 7:1</a>: “One does not exchange Second Tithe [money] on Sabbatical produce, but if it was done it should be eaten following the more stringent rules;” i. e., the rules of tithe and of Sabbatical, whichever happens to be more restrictive in a given situation. In this formulation, the discussion here is superfluous.</i>: “One does not buy Sabbatical [produce] with tithe money.” Rebbi Yose said, that is a disagreement<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Between Rebbi Simeon and the rabbis.</i>. Rebbi Jonah said, that is the opinion of everybody. The eaters of heave are careful<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Simeon permits heave from tithe only because Cohanim will be careful to finish all their heave in one sitting; then the problems discussed in the previous paragraph will not arise. But between Second Tithe and the Sabbatical, he will agree with the majority.</i>. Rebbi Ḥananiah objected before Rebbi Mana: Did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 9:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.9.8.1">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i> 9:7</a>.</i>: “If they<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Pesaḥ sacrifices. The Sages require that the animals be put out to graze until they develop a defect which makes them unfit for the altar. Then they can be sold and the money used for sacrifices. Rebbi Simeon notes that the ways in which Pesaḥ and firstlings are sacrificed are identical. Therefore, he permits a company of Cohanim, who are entitled to eat firstlings, to make a declaration that they intend to eat the one that should be Pesaḥ as such and the firstling as such, and eat both of them without knowing which is which. This means, he permits the firstling, usually eaten for two days and one night, to be eaten during one night only. He permits reducing the time allowed for consumption.</i> were mingled with firstlings, Rebbi Simeon says, if it is a company of Cohanim they should be eaten.” And we have stated on that, they should be eaten following the more stringent rules. He said to him, eaters of the Pesaḥ sacrifice in its time are as careful as the eaters of heave<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Nothing will be left beyond midnight.</i>. You should know that this is so since we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 1:14. Since increasing or decreasing the fire on the Sabbath is a capital crime, all food has to be fully cooked by the beginning of the Sabbath.</i>: “One roasts meat, onion, or egg only that they should be roasted”, but we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 1:15. If the first day of Passover is a Sabbath, the sacrifice, which must be roasted in the night, is put over the fire just at the start of the Sabbath and we are not afraid that somebody will increase or decrease the fire. This means that prohibitions of “fence” are unnecessary for the Pesaḥ sacrifice.</i>: “One lowers the Pesaḥ sacrifice into the oven at nightfall.”
Rebbi Joshua ben Levi says: One does add a fifth only for the first dedication<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">As a rule, any money paid to the Temple in liquidation of a personal debt is paid at the rate of 125% of the debt, the value plus one fifth from above; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 6:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.6.1.1"><i>Terumot</i> Chapter 6, Note 1</a>.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, this is what has been stated (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:27" href="/Leviticus.27.27"><i>Lev.</i> 27:27</a>): “If it is an impure animal, he shall redeem it for its value<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The verse continues: “its fifth he shall add to it”.</i>;” just as an impure animal is special in that it is a first dedication<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">An impure animal, i. e., a domesticated animal whose kind is not acceptable as a sacrifice, given to the Temple can only be an original gift, never a substitution.</i> so for anything which is a first dedication he has to add a fifth. Rebbi Samuel bar Ḥiyya bar Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">An Amora of the early second generation, student of R. Ḥanina. In the Babli, he appears as R. Samuel ben Ḥiyya.</i> in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: If well-being sacrificial animals bought with tithe money developed a blemish and one redeemed them, he adds a fifth. Rebbi Samuel bar Ḥiyya bar Jehudah in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: If well-being sacrificial animals bought with Passover money<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>pesaḥ</i> sacrifice must be eaten by subscription, if more animals were bought before the holiday than were actually used, the remainder are automatically used for well-being sacrifices (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 7b" href="/Zevachim.7b">Babli <i>Zebaḥim</i>7b</a>).</i> developed a blemish and one redeemed them, he adds a fifth. Rebbi Yudan said, one is needed and one is not needed. Rebbi Mana said, Passover is needed, well-being sacrifices are not needed<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Since tithe money is used mainly for well-being sacrifices, this is the original dedication.</i>. You should not say that since the <i>Pesaḥ</i> sacrifice is changed into well-being sacrifices it is like one dedication, he would not add a fifth<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">If the <i>pesaḥ</i> was automatically changed into a well-being sacrifice which then developed a blemish, one certainly would have to add a fifth. But in the case here the well-being sacrifice was bought with money from the redemption of a blemished <i>pesaḥ</i> sacrifice, one should not say that this is a second dedication exempt from the fifth.</i>.
Rebbi Huna said, the reason of Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.3.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>. This paragraph, for which the preceding one was the introduction, still should belong to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 3:2:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.3.2.2-4">Halakhah 2</a>,</i> is that he says the <i>pesaḥ</i> sacrifice cannot be substituted; if he substituted a second time it still is a <i>pesaḥ</i>. If he substituted, it is a well-being sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Substitution of sacrifices is forbidden (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:10" href="/Leviticus.27.10"><i>Lev</i>. 27:10</a>); if an animal was substituted, the verse prescribes that “the original and the substitute shall be holy.” It is not specified how to proceed if there are degrees of holiness.
