On this, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 9:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.9.7.1">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i> 9:6</a> states that if the substitution was before noon of the 14th of Nisan, when the <i>pesaḥ</i> could not have been slaughtered, the substituted animal cannot be slaughtered since nobody subscribed to it and it cannot become a well-being sacrifice since it is not a leftover <i>pesaḥ</i>. Therefore, it remains <i>pesaḥ</i> and must be left grazing until it develops a blemish when it must be sold and the money used for a well-being sacrifice. But if the substitution was after noontime of the 14th of Nisan, when the <i>pesaḥ</i> is slaughtered, the substitution is a leftover <i>pesaḥ</i> and automatically becomes a well-being sacrifice.</i>! Rebbi Mana said to him, did my teacher<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">This word is missing in the Rome ms.</i> not compare the following? Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, if well-being sacrifices bought with tithe money developed a blemish and he redeemed them, it<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The tithe money. As explained after this, the money now is profane and well-being offerings bought with this money are first dedications rather than second.</i> does not return to its original status to make it a second. Rebbi Zeïra, Rebbi Hila, both in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina, one said if well-being sacrifices were bought with tithe money, the holiness of tithe broke away from them<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Since in all respects, the rules of well-being sacrifices are more strict than those of tithe.</i>. If heave was bought with tithe money, the holiness of tithe did not break away from it<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.3.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>.</i>. The Mishnah breaks<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of R. Simeon in the Mishnah is faulty.</i>: Can you object from a case in which the holiness of tithe broke away to a case in which the holiness of tithe did not break away? The other one said, this does not mean one objects from a case in which its holiness broke away to a case in which its holiness did not break away; he says to him, it is one immersion<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The original argument of the Sages about the <i>ṭevul yom</i> is irrelevant since the important act was the immersion of the person; waiting a short time is not really a restriction.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody had coins in Jerusalem and his friend had produce<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The coins are tithe money, the produce is profane. By the declaration, the produce becomes Second Tithe and has to be eaten in purity but the coins are profane and may be used for business.</i>, he says to his friend, these coins are exchanged for your produce. That one has to eat his produce in purity but he can use his coins for his needs. But one should not say this to a vulgar unless they were from <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The vulgar cannot be trusted to eat anything in purity; see the Introduction to <i>Demay</i>. Second Tithe from <i>demay</i> is only conditional tithe; it is not sure whether it really has to be eaten in purity. Therefore, it may be exchanged for produce of the vulgar.</i>.<br>Produce in Jerusalem and coins in the countryside, he says these coins are exchanged for that produce. Coins in Jerusalem and produce in the countryside, he says that produce is exchanged for these coins; then the produce must be brought to and eaten in Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The only exchange prohibited is that of Second Tithe produce in Jerusalem.</i>.<br>[Tithe] money enters Jerusalem and leaves; [tithe] produce enters but does not leave. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, even produce enters and leaves.<br>If fully processed produce was transported through Jerusalem, its Second Tithe should be brought back and eaten in Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Even outside of Jerusalem it cannot be redeemed since tithes should have been separated before it was transported to Jerusalem.</i>. If the produce was not fully processed, the baskets of grapes are for the wine-press, and baskets of figs for the <i>muqẓeh</i>.<br>The House of Shammai say, the Second Tithe should be brought up and eaten in Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">In most Mishnah mss. this Mishnah is part of the preceding one and continues the preceding text. Even though no tithes were due at the time the produce was in Jerusalem, the influence of Jerusalem is so great that even potential tithe cannot be redeemed.</i>. But the House of Hillel say, it may be redeemed and eaten anywhere. Rebbi Simeon ben Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">A Tanna of the fifth generation, the most important student of R. Simeon bar Ioḥay. Sometimes, he is called R. Simeon ben Jehudah from Kefar Akko.</i> said in the name of Rebbi Yose, the House of Shammai and the House of Hillel did not disagree about produce which was not fully processed, that its Second Tithe may be redeemed and eaten anywhere. Where did they disagree? About fully processed produce, where the House of Shammay said, its Second Tithe should be brought back and eaten in Jerusalem, but the House of Hillel said, it may be redeemed and eaten anywhere. <i>Demay</i> enters, leaves, and may be redeemed<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is part of the statement of R. Simeon ben Jehudah. According to R. Simson and Maimonides in his Code (<i>Ma</i>‘<i>aser Šeni</i>2:9), it is agreed that tithe from <i>demay</i> produce which passed through Jerusalem may be redeemed anywhere but Second Tithe from <i>demay</i> may not be redeemed in Jerusalem. In his Commentary, Maimonides refers to Mishnah <i>Demay</i> 1:2 and frees Second Tithe of <i>demay</i> from the rules of Mishnaiot 4–7; this contradicts <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 3:3:7-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.3.3.7-9">Halakhah 6</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “But one should not say this to a vulgar unless they were from <i>demay</i>”; therefore, not if it is certain since one does not deliver certain [tithe] to a vulgar<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The vulgar cannot be trusted to eat anything in purity; see the Introduction to <i>Demay</i>. Second Tithe from <i>demay</i> is only conditional tithe; it is not sure whether it really has to be eaten in purity. Therefore, it may be exchanged for produce of the vulgar.</i>.
To make a dough and return it, to make a dough and return it<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 2:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.2.2.6">Chapter 2, Halakhah 4, Note 80</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, this means they made Jerusalem like a secure courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Ma</i>‘<i>serot</i> 2, Notes 100–102.</i>. Just as a secure courtyard induces <i>ṭevel</i>, this also induces <i>ṭevel</i>. Rebbi Jonah said, it would have been logical that even its houses would not induce <i>ṭevel</i> since they are property of all of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">In the Second Commonwealth, every house in Jerusalem was built with a servitude that its owner would host some pilgrims for the holidays. The owners were indemnified by their guests with the skins of their thanksgiving, well-being, and <i>pesaḥ</i> sacrifices. Therefore, there always were unrelated people who had the right to enter courtyard or house and, by Mishnah <i>Ma</i>‘<i>serot</i> 3:5, courtyard and house do not induce <i>ṭevel</i>.</i>. But it means they made Jerusalem like a secure courtyard. Just as a secure courtyard grabs, so this grabs<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Just as a secure courtyard “grabs” produce to require heave and tithes only if completely processed, so Jerusalem induces <i>ṭevel</i> for all produce only if completely processed. (Explanation of <i>Sefer Nir</i>.)</i>. Rebbi [Jonah]<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Missing in the mss. but required by the context, as noted by R. S. Cirillo.</i> said, this means if a heap was <i>ṭevel</i> for First and Second [Tithes] and he was warned [only] about the Second<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">At a moment where there was no obligation of Second Tithe since the First was not yet separated.</i> he is whipped. Rebbi Mana objected: Did we not state, the House of Shammai say, the Second Tithe should return and be eaten in Jerusalem? Can you say that he is whipped according to the House of Shammai<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Since only the House of Shammai recognize an action of Jerusalem on produce which is <i>ṭevel</i> for First Tithe, whipping somebody for not giving Second Tithe when he was warned about it before Second Tithe was due is ruling with the House of Shammai against the House of Hillel. This is unacceptable.</i>? But enclosures are more important; here also, enclosures are more important<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 3:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.3.2.3">Note 27</a>. The rules have nothing to do with Jerusalem being like a secure courtyard and those rules are irrelevant here. It is that for simple sacrifices and tithes the walls of Jerusalem happen to be the enclosure of the holy precinct; since biblical law requires the produce to be brought into the enclosure, its rules must be applied.</i>.
Rebbi Zeïra asked: If he separated Second Tithe for it from another place, is it freed or was it already grabbed by the enclosure<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">If fully processed produce not yet <i>ṭevel</i> was transported through Jerusalem, is it possible to give Second Tithe for it from other produce, which then could be redeemed, or, according to the anonymous Tanna of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.3.3.1">Mishnah 5</a>, must the Second Tithe be given from this batch only?</i>? Rebbi Jonah asked: If he made all of it Second Tithe for some place, is all of it taken or only one tenth of what is in it<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">There are two possible interpretations of this question. It is possible that he is of the opinion that, after First Tithe was given, the Second Tithe is already implicitly holy and therefore does not accept any other dedication since its place is not known, or that he holds that one tenth is reserved but the rest may be used for Second tithe at another place.</i>?
Therefore, baskets of table figs and baskets of table grapes are end of processing<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the Mishnah, that baskets of fruits for further processing are free from the duties of tithes.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The first part of the argument is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Maaser Sheni (Lieberman) 2:11" href="/Tosefta_Maaser_Sheni_(Lieberman).2.11">Tosephta 2:11</a>, in the name of R. Simeon ben Jehudah in the name of R. Yose. A second argument there (by the House of Hillel) is not in the text here.</i>“The House of Hillel said to the House of Shammai: Do you not agree with us that Second Tithe of not fully processed produce may be redeemed and eaten anywhere? Also with fully processed produce it is the same. The House of Shammai answered them: No. If you say this about not fully processed produce which one may abandon and thereby free from tithes, what can you say about fully processed produce which one cannot abandon and thereby free from tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Peah</i> 1:6 states that a person can give away any produce free of heave and tithes “until he smoothes the heap.” While the Yerushalmi there holds that this statement is from the House of Shammai, R. Simson points out that the House of Hillel will agree if “smoothing the heap” is interpreted as “completing processing in one’s courtyard or house.” But the situation envisaged in Mishnah 6/7 is that of produce fully processed but still not under the obligation of heave and tithes because it was processed in the field and, therefore, has to be transported to the house (via Jerusalem). Therefore, the House of Hillel will hold that produce fully processed but still not under the obligation of heave can be given away and then is not under any potential obligation of heave and tithes.</i>. The House of Hillel replied to them: Even fully processed produce one may abandon and thereby free from tithes; are not baskets of eating figs or grapes fully processed?” This proves that baskets of table figs and baskets of table grapes are end of processing<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">An amoraic note in the tannaïtic text to support the statement at the end of the preceding Halakhah.</i>. “The House of Shammai answered them: No. If you say this about not fully processed produce for which one may take Second Tithe from another place, what can you say about fully processed produce<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Which passed through Jerusalem. Since heave of the tithe can be taken from another place for other fully processed produce, certainly Second Tithe may be taken for it from another place.</i> for which one cannot take Second Tithe from another place!” This answers the question simply<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">R. Zeïra’s question in the preceding Halakhah which was not answered there.</i>.
Rebbi Zeïra said: Rebbi Ḥanina, Rebbi Jonathan, and Rebbi Joshua ben Levi ascended to Jerusalem. There happened to be produce<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">They obtained <i>ṭevel</i> produce and had to dispose of it according to the rules in the absence of a Temple.</i> for them; they wanted to redeem it in the countryside<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Take it outside the city as <i>ṭevel</i> and redeem the Second Tithe there, which they counld not do in the city.</i>. An old woman<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">The mss. read here סבא “an old man” but since the discussion is about the opinions of the old woman, one has to read סבתא here also. The Yerushalmi in general approves of women students of Jewish laws and tradition; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:4:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.4.12"><i>Berakhot</i> 3:4, Note 182</a>.</i> told them, your forefathers did not do that but took it outside of the wall and redeemed it there. The old woman thought one considers the enclosures as if they were risen; those rabbis thought one does not consider the enclosures as if they were risen<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">The old woman follows R. Joshua who declares (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Eduyot 8:6" href="/Mishnah_Eduyot.8.6">Mishnah <i>Idiut</i>8:6</a>) that one may eat Second Tithe inside the holy precinct even if the enclosures (the walls of Jerusalem) are destroyed. Therefore, it is enough to take the produce out of the part of the city that in Temple times was walled.<br>The rabbis hold that ritual Jerusalem is defined only by its walls; therefore, in the absence of walls there is no duty to keep the Second Tithe in Jerusalem but the city is defined by its actual extension, not that of Temple times. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 20a" href="/Makkot.20a">Babli (<i>Makkot</i> 20a</a>, in the name of Rabba or Rebbi Abba) chooses a third way, that holy food may be eaten only within actual walls but that the rules regarding “grabbing” are purely rabbinical and, therefore, abolished with the destruction of the sanctified walls.</i>. The old woman held with Rebbi Eliezer; those rabbis held with Rebbi Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The Yerushalmi holds that if R. Joshua thinks that the holiness of Solomon’s Temple is eternal, R. Eliezer must think that it terminated with the destruction of the Temple. The Babli (<i>Sebaḥim</i> 107b) tentatively disagrees, but then must hold that the enclosure of the Temple domain is merely cosmetic, a position not accepted in other parts of the Babli. The relationship of the positions of RR. Joshua and Eliezer to the problem at hand is tenuous since permanent sanctity is asserted only for the Temple domain on the Temple Mount, not for the rest of Jerusalem. See also the discussion of this problem in the author’s <i>Seder Olam</i> (Northvale NJ, 1998), pp. 257–259.<br>The text here is difficult; as R. M. Margalit points out it would seem that the old woman follows R. Joshua and the rabbis R. Eliezer. But that would put the rabbis on the wrong side of the law, a most unlikely situation. One has to conclude that the reference to a disagreement between RR. Joshua and Eliezer is to some <i>baraita</i> unknown to us.</i>. Rebbi Phineas made it impure and redeemed it; he took both into consideration.
Rebbi Jacob bar Idi and Rebbi Joshua ben Levi<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Probably one should read רבי יהושע instead of ורבי יהושע since the student, R. Jaacob bar Idi, can be a tradent of the teachings but not the equal of his teacher.</i>, practice follows the student<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon ben Jehudah in his statement about <i>demay</i>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.3.3.1">Note 60</a>.</i>. Rebbi Zeïra said, only for produce which is <i>ṭevel</i> as <i>demai.</i> But [tithe of] <i>demai</i> itself is already grabbed by the enclosure.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A tree which stands inside<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon ben Jehudah in his statement about <i>demay</i>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.3.3.1">Note 60</a>.</i> and [its crown] extends outside, or stands outside and extends inside, what is above the wall and inside is like inside, above the wall and outside is like outside. Oil presses whose entrances are inside but their space extends to the outside or whose entrances are outside but their space extends to the inside, the House of Shammai say it is all counted as inside, but the House of Hillel say what is under the wall and inside is like inside, under the wall and outside is like outside<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">In all these rules, the wall appears both outside and inside. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:12:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.12.1">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i> 7:10</a> it is stated that the wall is counted as inside. Therefore, the interpretation here should be: From the wall to the outside it is outside. However, in the Tosephta (<i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i> 2:15) the status of the wall is a matter of controversy.</i>.<br>If chambers are built in the holy precinct and open to the profane domain, their insides are profane but their roofs holy<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">In the profane domain, Second Tithe and simple sacrifices (family sacrifices) may be consumed. In the holy part, most-holy sacrifices may be consumed and simple sacrifices be slaughtered. (Most-holy sacrifices must be slaughtered inside the precinct, North of the altar.)</i>. If they are built in the profane domain but open to the holy precinct, their insides are holy but their roofs profane. If they are built in the holy and the profane and open both to the holy and the profane<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">The chamber of the fireplace, where the Cohanim kept watch during the night and from which a Cohen who became impure could first descend to a subterranean <i>miqweh</i> and then leave directly into the profane domain as a <i>ṭevul yom.</i></i>, their insides and their roofs, over the holy and in direction of the holy they are holy<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The border between holy and profane domain was indicated by a mosaic strip. This strip takes the function of the wall which belongs to both domains.</i>, over the profane and in direction of the profane they are profane.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Eleazar said, for restriction. Rebbi Yose asked, what means “for restriction”? Rebbi Jonah said, that is what we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">The text here is hopelessly garbled since oil presses (Mishnah 8) are in the city, not on the Temple Mount but sacrifices are slaughtered in the Temple precinct (Mishnah 9). As a minimum one would have to replace “slaughter” by “eat” to adapt the text to the circumstances of the city of Jerusalem.<br>A consistent text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Maaser Sheni (Lieberman) 2:12" href="/Tosefta_Maaser_Sheni_(Lieberman).2.12">Tosephta 2:12</a>: “Oil presses whose entrances are inside [the city] but their space extends to the outside or whose entrances are outside but their space extends to the inside, the House of Shammai say one does not redeem there Second Tithe as if it were inside and not does not eat simple sacrifices as if it were outside. But the House of Hillel say what is under the wall and inside is like inside, under the wall and outside is like outside. Rebbi Yose said, this is the teaching of Rebbi Aqiba. The earlier teaching: The House of Shammai say one does not redeem there Second Tithe as if it were inside nor does one eat simple sacrifices as if it were outside. But the House of Hillel say, they are similar to chambers; those open to the inside are like inside, those open to the outside like outside.”<br>It is impossible to correct the text by the Tosephta (except for “eat” instead of “slaughter”) since the statement of R. Yose is quoted in a different context at the end of the Halakhah. Also, to extend the prohibition both of eating sacrifices and of redeeming tithe to the entire space would do violence to the text; in that case there would be no difference between being open to the inside or the outside. One must conclude that the place where one restricts to exclude the activities both outside and inside the walls is the place under the wall itself.<br>Maimonides (<i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i> 2:16) formulates as follows: “Houses by the wall which open inside the wall but extend outside, under the wall and inside it is like inside the city in all respects, but under the wall and outside one does neither eat nor redeem as a restriction. If they were built inside but open to the outside, from the wall to the outside one redeems, to the inside one neither redeems nor eats as a restriction. The thickness of the wall and the loopholes are like the inside.” The last sentence is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:12:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.12.1">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i>7:10</a>.</i>: “Oil presses whose doors are open inside but which extend to the outside, under the wall and inside is like inside, under the wall and outside is like outside. One does not slaughter there simple sacrifices as one would inside and one does not redeem Second Tithe as outside. If their doors are open outside but extend to the inside, under the wall and outside is like outside, under the wall and inside, one does not slaughter there simple sacrifices as one would inside and one does not redeem Second Tithe as outside.”
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, so is the Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">He adds one word, “is holy”, to the last clause in the Mishnah. This is all that is quoted from the Mishnah.</i>: “Their insides are holy and their roofs (are profane)<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The word is missing in the Rome ms.; in the Leyden ms. it is added by a second hand. Most probably it should be disregarded; as it was by R. S. Cirillo.</i>, over the holy and in the direction of the holy they are holy, over the profane and in direction of the profane they are profane.” “Built in the holy but open to the holy and the profane, the inside is holy. Built in the profane but open to the holy and the profane, the inside is profane. There, where you say the inside is holy one eats most holy sacrifices, slaughters simple holy sacrifices and the impure who enters is guilty.”
Rav Jehudah in the name of Rav: One whips only for 187 length by 135 width<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The inner measurements in cubits of the enclosure of the Temple as stated in Mishnah <i>Middot</i>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 55b" href="/Zevachim.55b">Babli, <i>Zebaḥim</i> 55b</a>, the statement of Rav Jehudah is in the name of his teacher Samuel.</i>. But did we not state: In a chamber built flush with the wall of the Temple courtyard one eats most holy sacrifices, slaughters simple holy sacrifices, but an impure person who enters there cannot be punished<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">This supports Rav but contradicts the <i>baraita</i> quoted in the preceding paragraph.</i>. Explain it following the Tanna who stated<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Tosephta 2:12, Note 88. The preceding <i>baraita</i> follows the Sages, the one quoted here R. Aqiba.</i>: “Rebbi Yose said, these are the words of Rebbi Aqiba. But the Sages say, they classified the chambers and all chambers follow their openings.”

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Second Tithe which entered Jerusalem and became impure, whether from original impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">One of the biblical sources of impurity such as a dead person, a person suffering from a genital discharge, a cadaver, etc.</i> or from derivative impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">By touching something impure in original impurity. Most, but not all, derivative impurities are rabbinic; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1"><i>Demay</i> 2, Notes 136–137</a>.</i>, inside [the city] or outside, the House of Shammai say, all should be redeemed and eaten inside except what became impure from original impurity outside<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Since by biblical law it could not be eaten when brought into Jerusalem, the enclosure never “grabbed” it (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 3:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.3.3.4">Note 63</a>).</i>. But the House of Hillel say, all should be redeemed and may be eaten outside except what became impure from derivative impurity inside<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">If its impurity is rabbinic in character, the biblical law will not allow it to be taken out of Jerusalem.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:24" href="/Deuteronomy.14.24"><i>Deut</i>. 14:24</a>): “For you will be unable to take it.” Where do we hold? If far from the Place<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Shiloh or Jerusalem.</i>, it already is written: “If [the distance] will be too much for you.<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">“(24) But if the distance is too much for you, for you will be unable to take it, for the Place, chosen by the Eternal, your God, to put His Name there, is too far from you, because the Eternal blessed you - (25) then turn it <i>into money</i>, bundle that money in your hand and go to the Place which will be chosen by the Eternal, your God. (26) Then <i>spend the money</i> for all you desire …”<br>The <i>Sifry Deut</i>. (107) explains that while <i>the distance</i> is spatial distance, <i>too far</i> is temporal distance. In any case, the clause <i>unable to carry</i> is redundant.</i>” If near the Place, it already is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:26" href="/Deuteronomy.14.26"><i>Deut</i>. 14:26</a>): “You shall spend the money<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">At the Place one is restricted to spending the money; the exchange of tithe for money is restricted to places far away by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:25" href="/Deuteronomy.14.25">v. 25</a>.</i>.” What does it mean “for you will be unable to take it”? You cannot redeem it<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Since the verse cannot deal with a situation far from the Place it must be at the Place. The only case in which one cannot take tithe brought to the Place is if the tithe became impure and, therefore, inedible as tithe. Then one has to spend the [redemption] money.<br>The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 36b" href="/Pesachim.36b">Babli (<i>Pesaḥim</i> 36b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 53a" href="/Bava_Metzia.53a"><i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 53a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 19b" href="/Makkot.19b"><i>Makkot</i> 19b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 112b" href="/Sanhedrin.112b"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 112b</a>) has a weird derivation of the same result from the same verse. The Babylonian identification of שׂאת as “food” contrasts with the Yerushalmi’s identification (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Chagigah 1:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Chagigah.1.3.2"><i>Ḥagigah</i> 1:3</a>, fol. 76b) as “gift”.</i>, and it is written: “You shall spend the money.”
It was stated: Bar Qappara said, “original impurity” is biblical, “derivative impurity” rabbinic<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">In the interpretation of this Mishnah.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, in both cases it is biblical. The House of Shammai are difficult for Rebbi Joḥanan: “The House of Shammai say, all should be redeemed and eaten inside except what became impure from original impurity outside;” what is the difference between original impurity outside and derivative impurity outside; are not both biblical? Are the words of the House of Hillel not also difficult? “The House of Hillel say, all should be redeemed and may be eaten outside except what became impure from derivative impurity inside”; what is the difference between original impurity inside and derivative impurity inside; are not both biblical? The rabbis discuss only that from Bar Qappara.
It is difficult, according to Bar Qappara for the House of Shammai, since “the House of Shammai say, all should be redeemed and eaten inside except what became impure from original impurity outside;” what is the difference between derivative impurity and original impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">This clause should be deleted, it is copied from the previous text and makes no sense here. The Rome text, הַכֹּל יִפָּדֶה וְיֵאָכֵל בִּפְנִים בֵּין מִשֶּׁנִּיטְמָא בְְּאַב הַטּוּמְאָה בַּחוּץ בֵּין בְּאַב הַטוּמְאָה is not better.</i>; what is the difference between outside and inside, are not both biblical? That one should not say, is that not Second Tithe which enters and leaves? If it is so, it should not be redeemed, so that one should not say, is that not Second Tithe which enters Jerusalem and is redeemed<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Which also is forbidden for pure tithe.</i>? If it became impure inside, the enclosure had grabbed it; if it became impure outside, the enclosure never grabbed it<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Since it never could be eaten inside the sanctified area.</i>. And is it not difficult even for the House of Hillel? For “the House of Hillel say, all should be redeemed and may be eaten outside except what became impure from derivative impurity inside”; what is the difference for derivative impurity between outside and inside; are not both rabbinic? When he brought it inside it was on condition it should not be “grabbed” by the enclosure<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>actually</i> it never could be eaten inside the sanctified area.</i>.
Rebbi Zeïra said, this means<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Since rabbinically impure tithe is pure by biblical standards, it seems that a mental reservation of the farmer can override the biblical decree that tithe brought into the holy enclosure has to be consumed as such.</i> pure Second Tithe which he brought inside on condition that the enclosure not “grab” it, the enclosure does not “grab” it. Rebbi Jonah asked, it is pure and you say so? But it must be: If he transgressed and redeemed it, it is redeemed<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">If he made a mental reservation and then redeemed the tithe in Jerusalem, the transaction is valid since the prohibition of redemption is a rabbinic interpretation of the biblical verse. This supports the position of Maimonides in his <i>Sefer Hammiẓwot</i> that rules derived from biblical verses by rabbinic interpretation are rabbinic in character.</i>.
Rebbi Jacob the Southerner asked before Rebbi Yose: If it became impure outside of Jerusalem and was brought inside, it should not leave lest one say, we saw Second Tithe entering Jerusalem and leaving<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">This is a question about the House of Shammai’s position which in this case requires the tithe to be removed from Jerusalem. The answer is that this case is so rare that it would be dealt with in a rabbinic court in public whereas redemption of tithe which became impure is frequent, done in private, and unremarkable.</i>? Leaving is publicized, redemption is not publicized.
Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada inquired before Rebbi Mana: If it became impure by derivative impurity and he redeemed it<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">As required. However, since the tithe is still pure by biblical standards, the redemption is <i>de facto</i>, not <i>de jure.</i></i>, then it became impure in original impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Now it has to be redeemed by biblical decree. If the original money is still completely in the farmer’s hands, he now turns the rabbinic redemption into a biblical one.</i>; do we say that if the original money is still there he redeems with it, otherwise he cannot redeem with it, and one is whipped neither for the first nor the second monies<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">If he misappropriates the monies and spends them outside of Jerusalem, as described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.1.3.1">Mishnah 1:5</a>, he cannot be prosecuted if the produce remains rabbinically impure since the redemption is not biblical; nor can he be prosecuted if the produce becomes biblically impure and is redeemed by new money because the second redemption is a formality only since the produce was unusable as tithe.</i>? Rebbi Jonah asked, is it the same for him who buys<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.1.3.1">Mishnaiot 1:5</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.1.3.1">6</a>. There, it is spelled out that produce bought with tithe money outside of Jerusalem cannot be redeemed. Its status is at least as restricted as that of original tithe brought into the enclosure of Jerusalem.</i>? Rebbi Mana said, the enclosure “grabs” and the buyer “grabs”. What was said for the enclosure was said for the buyer.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If what was bought with tithe money became impure, it should be redeemed. Rebbi Jehudah says, it should be buried<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">It is holy but cannot be eaten. It cannot be left to rot since one might eat from it inadvertently. This Mishnah is quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 38a" href="/Pesachim.38a">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 38a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 113a" href="/Sanhedrin.113a"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 113a</a>, <i>Baba Meẓiʻa</i> 53b, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 49b" href="/Zevachim.49b"><i>Zebaḥim</i> 49b</a>.</i>. They said to Rebbi Jehudah, if original Second Tithe which became impure is redeemed, what was bought with tithe money and became impure certainly should be redeemed. He said to them, no! If you said about original Second Tithe, which can be redeemed when it is pure and far from the Place, can you say the same about what was bought with tithe money which cannot be redeemed when it is pure and far from the Place?<br>If a deer bought with tithe money died, it should be buried in its hide. Rebbi Simeon says, it should be redeemed<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">This is explained in the Halakhah.</i>. If he bought it alive and slaughtered it, if it became impure it should be redeemed<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">For the anonymous Tanna and R. Jehudah, it is like any other food that became impure. R. Yose extends the rules of the preceding case to this one, since a deer never can be a sacrifice.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, it should be buried. If he bought it slaughtered<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">This use is fully approved by the biblical verse.</i> and it became impure, he treats it following the rules of produce.<br>If somebody lends flasks<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">The mss. of the Maimonides tradition have more accurately קנקניו “his own flasks”; the vintner gives one of his flasks to put in tithe wine since the wine has to be in some vessel. Before filling the wine into the flasks, he specified that the flasks were a loan to the tithe. Therefore, he does not have to redeem them, i. e., to buy food in the value of the flasks to eat in the holiness of tithe.</i> for Second Tithe, even if he closed the top with clay<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">A semi-permanent seal; in this case it is needed to transport the wine to Jerusalem. Flasks were usually sealed in this way only for sale and transport.</i>, tithe did not acquire it. If he filled them without saying anything<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">He did not specify that the flask would be filled with tithe wine.</i>, before he closed the top with clay<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">He declared the wine to be Second Tithe. But since the flask was sealed, if he makes no special declaration, he sanctifies the flask with its contents.</i>, tithe did not acquire it; after he closed the top with clay, tithe acquired it. Before he closed the top with clay, one may lift by 101<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">If heave accidentally fell into one of the flasks and it is not known where it fell, if the profane is more that 101 times the heave, the latter can be lifted following the rules of heave.</i>, after he closed the top with clay any one sanctifies<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Since sealed flasks usually are prepared for sale, they fall under the rules of items sold singly which never can become insignificant and even one sanctified among 1000 profane makes all sanctified; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Orlah 3:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Orlah.3.5.1">Mishnah <i>Orlah</i> 3:7</a>.</i>. Before he closed the top with clay, he gives heave from one for all, after he closed the top with clay he has to give heave from each single one.<br><sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">This Mishnah is a continuation of the previous one. If somebody sealed the mouths of his pitchers with clay, how can he undo what he did?</i> The House of Shammai say, he opens and pours into a vat, but the House of Hillel say, he opens and does not have to pour. Where has this been said? At a place where usually one sells sealed, but at a place where usually one sells open, the flask did not become profane<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, he opened the flask for redemption because everybody does it for sale; this has no influence on the status of holiness of either the flask or its contents.</i>. However, if he is meticulous to sell by volume<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Even if he sells whole barrels he will measure exactly the amount it contains and does not use an approximate formula. In that case, no vessel is ever part of a sale unless paid for separately; also for his redemption of tithes the vessel will never be counted.</i>, the flask did become profane. Rebbi Simeon said<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of R. Simeon refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.1.2.1">Mishnah 1:3</a>, that if wine usually is sold in sealed amphoras, the amphora is profane even though paid for with tithe money. R. Simeon adds that even if wine is sold in open amphoras, a stipulation may make the amphora profane. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Maaser Sheni (Lieberman) 2:18" href="/Tosefta_Maaser_Sheni_(Lieberman).2.18">Tosephta (2:18</a>) shows that R. Simeon reduces the disagreement between the Houses of Hillel and Shammai to the case when all flasks are still in the wine cellar near the vat from which they were filled.</i>, also if somebody says to another person, I am selling to you this amphora except its flask, the flask became profane.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> What is the reason of Rebbi Jehudah? The first money, not second money<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:25" href="/Deuteronomy.14.25"><i>Deut.</i> 14:25</a>: “then turn it <i>into the money</i>.” The definite article is taken to restrict the money to the coins originally given. This argument is described in the Babli, <i>Baba Meẓiʻa</i>45a, as that of the House of Shammai. This is possible since R. Jehudah was a student of his father R. Ilaï, a student of R. Eliezer who started out as a disciple of the House of Shammai. The House of Hillel (the anonymous majority in Mishnah 11) hold that the repetition in the verse, “then turn it into <i>money</i>, bundle the <i>money</i> in your hand” means that monies can be exchanged; otherwise it would have said “bundle it”.</i>. Only because they objected to him by an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i>, he answered in terms of an argument <i>de minore ad majus.</i>
Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: They treated deer like dedications for the upkeep of the Temple to require standing and appraisal<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">The rules of dedications of animals whose monetary equivalent should be used for the upkeep of the Temple are spelled out in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27" href="/Leviticus.27"><i>Lev.</i> 27</a>. The relevant verses are 11–12 and 27. (11) “If it is any unclean animal which cannot be a sacrifice to the Eternal, one should make the animal stand before a Cohen. (12) The Cohen should appraise it, whether it is good or bad; the Cohen’s appraisal shall stand.” (27) “If it is an unclean animal, he should redeem it for its appraisal ..” Since an unclean (nonkosher) animal <i>never</i> can be a sacrifice, the “unclean” animal of v. 11 must be a kosher animal with a blemish, which <i>now</i> cannot be a sacrifice. V. 27 therefore details the rules of nonkosher animals dedicated to the Temple. The rabbis hold that the parallel expressions “unclean animal” indicate that the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:11-12" href="/Leviticus.27.11-12">vv. 11–12</a> can be transferred to v. 27 but R. Simeon holds that in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:27" href="/Leviticus.27.27">v. 27</a> no “standing” is mentioned, therefore it is not needed. (Details in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 32b" href="/Temurah.32b">Babli <i>Temurah</i>32b</a>).<br>The rabbis hold that any dedicated animal which cannot stand before a Cohen cannot be appraised and, therefore, cannot be redeemed. R. Simeon holds that animals which never can be sacrifices, nonkosher animals and kosher wild animals, do not need standing; they can be redeeemed even if dead.</i>.
Rebbi Jeremiah asked before Rebbi Zeïra: Does an unclean animal need standing and appraisal? He said to him, if Rebbi Yose had said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan that a pure wild animal does not need standing and appraisal, an unclean animal would not need it. Rebbi Hila said, we have stated thus (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:21" href="/Leviticus.27.21"><i>Lev</i>. 27:21</a>)<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Beḥuqotay Paras̊ah</i> 4(3), a similar text; quoted in Babli <i>Baba Meẓiʻa</i> 54b.</i>: “If an unclean animal, he should redeem it as appraised<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">The verse adds: “He has to add its fifth”.</i>.” Just as an unclean animal is particular in that it is the same at the time of its redemption as at the time of its dedication<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">It could not be a sacrifice in either case; it was alive in both cases.</i>, so I am adding the dead animal which is the same at the time of its redemption as at the time of its dedication, and I exclude the one for which he said, this is dedicated, and then it died, which is not the same at the time of its redemption as at the time of its dedication<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Even according to the Sages, it can be redeemed without standing and appraisal.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, a Mishnah said so<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">This is the operative rule.</i>: “One commits larceny with a donkey and its milk<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Meїlah</i> 3:5. Larceny committed on anything dedicated to the upkeep of the Temple incurs a fine of 25% and requires a sacrifice for atonement.</i>.” Is not milk comparable to its being dead<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">The use of masculine חמור for a female donkey is in all Mishnah mss.; it has wrongly been corrected in the editions of Babli (<i>Meїlah</i> 12b).</i>? With all that needs redemption one can commit larceny<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">It was not there at the moment of dedication and, being a fluid, it cannot stand.</i>. You cannot explain it as giving the rules of dead [animals] since we stated “a donkey”<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">A live one.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina said before Rebbi Mana, explain it following Rebbi Simeon since Rebbi Simeon said, dedications for the upkeep of the Temple do not need standing and appraisal. He said to him, if it is following Rebbi Simeon, why a donkey and not any animal<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">It could even have been a blemished kosher animal.</i>?
Rebbi Zeïra said, the Torah said, spend it in the Holy Place and assemble it in the countryside. Just as in the holy place the flask is profane<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.1.2.1">Mishnah 1:4</a>, if wine is bought in Jerusalem with tithe money, the value of the flask does not have to be estimated and an equivalent value eaten in the holiness of tithe. The argument gives the rationale for the first clause of the Mishnah.</i>, in the contryside can the flask be grasped by tithe?
Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, so is the Mishnah: “If before he sealed the top with clay he gave it its name<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">He declared the contents of the flask to be Second Tithe.</i>, tithe did not acquire it; if after he sealed the top with clay he gave it its name, tithe acquired it. If before he sealed the top with clay he gave it its name, one may lift by 101, if after he sealed the top with clay any one sanctifies. If before he sealed the top with clay he gave it its name, he gives heave from one for all, if after he sealed the top with clay he gave it its name, he has to give heave from each single one.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Maaser Sheni (Lieberman) 2:18" href="/Tosefta_Maaser_Sheni_(Lieberman).2.18">Tosephta 2:18</a> enumerates fish sauce, vinegar, <i>muries</i>, oil, and (date) honey. Fish sauce and <i>muries</i> cannot be subject to tithe. Maimonides in his Code (<i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i>8:5) copies the entire list. In both mss. and the <i>editio princeps</i> of the Tosephta, “tithe acquired it”; but Maimonides (<i>loc. cit.</i>) copies “tithe did not acquire it.” It is impossible to determine whether or not the Tosephta is corrupt in this passage.</i> When has this been said? For wine; but for oil, whether he closed the top with clay or did not close the top with clay, tithe did not acquire it. Whether he closed the top with clay or did not close the top with clay, he may lift by 101. Whether he closed the top with clay or did not close the top with clay, he gives heave from one for all.
Rebbi Ḥananiah said, it is difficult about the House of Shammai! What is the difference between this and five sacks on a threshing floor? For five [separate] sacks on one threshing floor, can one not give heave and tithe from one for the other?
Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, it refers back to the first [part]. Rebbi Ba said, to the second<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody agrees that Mishnah 14 refers to Mishnah 13. The question is only, does it refer to the first part, do the House of Shammai oppose the statement that a prior declaration leaves the flask as tithe money unless the wine was entirely put in order in the vat, or does it refer to the second part, that the wine was poured without any specification.</i>.
“If he said, a <i>quartarius</i> of profane is in this barrel, the barrel is profane<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">A similar statement is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Maaser Sheni (Lieberman) 2:18" href="/Tosefta_Maaser_Sheni_(Lieberman).2.18">Tosephta 2:18</a>: “When was it said that [the flask] became tithe money? If all was tithe. But if he added a <i>quartarius</i> (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:5:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.5.10"><i>Berakhot</i> 3, Note 227</a>) of profane [wine], the tithe did not acquire it whether sealed with clay or not.”</i>.” Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The Mishnah says so, “Rebbi Simeon said, also if somebody says to another person, I am selling to you this amphora except its flask, the flask became profane.<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Any reservation in this situation is valid. R. Joḥanan accepts the statement of R. Simeon as practice even though it is presented as the opinion of only one person because no opposition is noted.</i>”

Chapter 4



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody transports Second Tithe produce from a place of high prices to one of low prices<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">. For some reason, Maimonides translates “from a clean to a dirty place”, implying that dirty places have cheap prices, clean places high prices.</i> or vice versa, he redeems at the level of his place<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">At the place where the redemption is actually taking place, outside Jerusalem.</i>. If he brings produce from the threshing floor to town or from the wine-press to town, the excess value<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The difference between produce, etc., in town and out in the fields is added to Second Tithe money but the cost of transportation from the field to town is on him and cannot be deducted from tithe money.</i> belongs to the Second but the expense is on him.<br>One redeems Second Tithe following the low rate at which the grocer buys<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">At wholesale.</i>, not at which he sells; the one at which the banker gives small coins, not at which he assembles large coins<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The banker engaged in exchange operations makes money on the spread between buying and selling rates. For redeeming tithe one values bronze coins as if one were a banker selling copper coins against silver.</i>. One does not redeem Second Tithe <i>en bloc</i><sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.5.1"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 2, Note 198</a>.</i>; if the price is known it is redeemed by the information of one person, if the price is not known by the information of three, as e. g., wine getting sour, fruit rotting, or coins rusting.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody transports Second Tithe produce,” etc. Rebbi Mana said, they said only “if somebody transports”; therefore originally it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">. If people were encouraged to transport heave from place to place (except on the way to Jerusalem), the Mishnah would have been formulated: מוליכין פירות “One transports produce”. The formulation: If somebody does it, then … is only used for actions which are disapproved of but which are not actually forbidden.</i>. This is about Second Tithe produce, but for produce which is <i>ṭevel</i> for Second Tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Produce for which heave and First Tithe had already been given.</i> it is originally permitted, following this: Rebbi had produce here and in Batanaia<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Ma‘serot</i> 4, Note 82.</i>; he fixed the tithes of here there and redeemed them according to the going price there.
It was stated: Whether it was expensive and became cheap or cheap and became more expensive<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the <i>baraita</i> permits redeeming at the lowest rate between the time Second Tithe was given and the time it was redeemed.</i>. We understand “expensive and became cheap<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Since in this case, the price of redemption is the actual price at the time of redemption.</i>”. “Cheap and became more expensive”? There is a difference because he could use a trick and free it from the fifth<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.4.3.1">Mishnah 4</a>, if a third party redeems the tithe, he does not have to add a fifth.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Maaser Sheni (Lieberman) 3:1" href="/Tosefta_Maaser_Sheni_(Lieberman).3.1">Tosephta, 3:1</a>, R. Joshua ben Qorḥa states the opposite rule: <i>Demay</i> follows the rules of the Mishnah, but certain produce always is redeemed by the higher tariff.</i>: “Abba Ḥilfai the small-town man<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 123a" href="/Bava_Batra.123a">Babli, <i>Baba Batra</i> 123a</a>, there appears a אבא חליפא קרויא, Abba Ḥalifa Small-Towner, who, however, was a student of R. Ḥiyya bar Abba and cannot be identical with the person mentioned here.</i> said, when has this been said? For certain [produce], but for <i>demay</i> whether it was expensive and became cheap or was cheap and became expensive, he may sell it cheaply.” Why? Is it because it always may be sold cheaply or because it may not be returned to its place<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">So that it always could be redeemed at the cheaper place.</i>? What is the difference? If later it rose in price again at its place<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">At the moment of redemption there is no cheap place.</i>. If you say, because it may not be returned to its place, did it not increase in value? If you say, because it always may be sold cheaply also in this case it can be sold cheaply. Like this: Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba was in Rome and he saw them redeem there Nicolaos dates<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:5:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.5.4"><i>Berakhot</i> 6, Note 162</a>; <i>Demay</i> 2, Notes 15,16.</i> from here at the price here<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">At the much lower rate of the Land of Israel.</i>. He said, who taught them? Ḥilfai the small-town man taught them.
It was stated: A person<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">A money changer or money dealer. This paragraph should belong to <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i>.</i> may earn up to a <i>šeqel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">2 (silver) denars per gold denar? or aureus? The rate of 2000 denars to one aureus points to the early fourth Century.</i>, a person may earn up to a quarter<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">A quarter of a tetradrachma, one denar. Probably the larger amount is for retail transactions, the smaller for wholesale.</i>. How is this done<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Without transgressing the interest prohibitions.</i>? A denar here is worth 2000, but in Arbel 2000 and a <i>laqan</i><sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">A word of uncertain origin and meaning. Following M. Zuckermann, <i>Talmudische Münzen und Gewichte</i>, Breslau 1862, most Talmudic Dictionaries derive this word from Greek λευκόν “a white [thing]”, supposed to mean “a small silver coin”. (Jastrow, deriving the word from the root לקי “to hit, smite”, takes it as translation of Greek λεπτόν, “a small copper coin.”)<br>However, there never was a Greek coin λευκόν (whose transliteration moreover would have to be לבקן or לוקן)- The efforts of H. J. Sheftel (ערך מלין לשעורי תורה Berdičev 1907, p. 39b/c) and D. Sperber (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 4:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.4.1.5">Note 24</a>) to determine the monetary value of the supposed coin are inconclusive.<br>The apparent meaning of לקן here and in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 4:1:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.4.1.2-5"><i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 4:1,</a> fol. 9c, “a small indeterminate amount of money” rather suggests a connection with Arabic لَقَن “small dish, cup” which is used in the composite عِوَض لقن “small thing given in exchange”, or “trifle given as agio”.<br>{Perhaps cf. Greek λεκάνη, ἡ and λέκος, -εως, τό, “dish, pot, pan” from which a late diminutive λεκίσκιον, τό, “small measure or weight” (E. G.)}.</i>. He wants to give 50 myriads<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">500’000 denar = 250 aurei.</i> and brings it up, here by 2000 and in Arbel by 2[000] and <i>laqan</i><sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. also D. Sperber, <i>Roman Palestine 200–400</i>, <i>Money and Prices</i>, Ramat-Gan 1974, Chap. 14.</i>.
It was stated: One does not redeem Second Tithe as Second Tithe but as profane<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Unless the person who redeems states that the produce shall be profane and the holiness of tithe transferred to the coins, there is no transfer.</i>. Rebbi Saul asked: Think of it, if everybody knew that it was Second<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">What else could he do with it? Even so, there is no transfer without declaration.</i>? Even so. It was stated: One redeems Second Tithe only from its own kind<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Tithes do not have to be given from earmarked produce; the farmer may give tithe from one part of his harvest for all other produce of the same harvest but at other places.</i>. Otherwise, what would we say? One might redeem wheat for barley, barley for wheat; even fine wheat for coarse wheat<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Peah</i> 2, Notes 85,86.</i>, coarse wheat for fine wheat.
Rebbi Ḥanania said: Rebbi took early ripe green melons<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.2.1"><i>Kilaim</i> 1:2, Note 38</a>.</i> for the government<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">To invite (Augustus or Caesar) Antoninus, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 9:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.9.3.3"><i>Kilaim</i> 9:4, Note 79</a>.</i> and fixed their Second Tithe in each peduncle<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">He then redeemed them together.</i>. He considered them as if cut<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The peduncle with some fruit flesh cut off from the melon.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan asked, they were whole and you say cut? Rebbi Jonah said, that is correct! If two people were partners in a green melon, one having one part and the other two, can he say, take your part and I shall take mine? But for the two together it is sold more dearly; here also together it is sold<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., redeemed.</i> more dearly. Like this: Rebbi Simeon ben Rebbi commanded those Southerners who were disregarding it<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">They disregarded Second Tithe (which without a Temple cannot be used for anything) and which, at least for produce other than grain, wine, and olive oil, was rabbinic even in the Times of the Temple.</i>. Bar Qappara lifted them and before him cut them into little pieces. He said to him, is that worth anything<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">If it has no monetary value, it is not subject to tithes.</i>? So far, some things are cut into little pieces and they are no good, other things are cut into little pieces and are good<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of Rebbi against Bar Qappara.</i>.
Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, one redeems Second Tithe only up to thirty six times [the coin’s] worth. And Rebbi Ḥizqiah said, one who exchanges should not exchange for half a <i>peruṭah</i> so he should not be like one who exchanges for a blank<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>peruṭa</i> was an Hasmonean copper coin; in Talmudic times it was purely a unit of computation. According to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:2-33" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.2-33">Yerushalmi <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:1</a> (fol. 58d), one <i>as</i>(<i>obolus</i>) was counted as 6 <i>peruṭot.</i> The statement then means that for redemption purposes, a <i>denar</i> can be represented by a <i>peruṭah</i>.<br>Half a <i>peruṭah</i> never was an official coin; for blanks cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.1.1.1">Chapter 1, Note 45</a>.</i>; it must be worth a <i>peruṭah</i>.
Rebbi Immi said, Kama came before Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. They said to him, go and pay according to those Tannaїm: “One redeems Second Tithe when it is cheap, not when it is expensive; at the rate at which the grocer buys, not at which he sells; the one at which the banker gives small coins, not at which he assembles large coins.” So far for large quantities, what about small ones? Even so, at the rate at which the grocer buys, not at which he sells; the one at which the banker gives small coins, not at which he assembles large coins.
Rebbi Naḥman bar Jacob<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The only known Naḥman bar Jacob is Rav Naḥman bar Jacob, Chief Judge in Babylonia and son-in-law of the Resh Galuta. Since carpenters do not make baskets from leaves, they were certainly incompetent to testify to their volume or value. The story therefore notes that in Babylonia, where tithes are rabbinic practice only, any estimate is good.</i> showed a basket of palm leaves to a carpenter and redeemed following his opinion. Rebbi Yannai showed a quarter [<i>qab</i>] wheat to a wheat dealer<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">In the Rome ms. סיטונאי “grain wholesaler”. While the Mishnah takes as standard the selling price of the wholesaler, he asked his buying price.</i> and redeemed following his opinion. Rebbi Simon showed produce to Rebbi Ḥilqiah who asked him, by their true value? He answered, yes. Rebbi Ḥilqiah said in the name of Rebbi Simon, one does not redeem Second Tithe following the opinion of imbeciles. Rebbi Phineas showed produce to a grits-maker who asked him, by their true value? He said to him, so said Rebbi Ḥilqiah in the name of Rebbi Simon, one does not redeem following the opinion of imbeciles. Rebbi Shammai said, how much a person wants [to pay] on a clear noontime at the summer solstice when the bath master<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">He runs a thermal bath which will not have many customers in the summer heat; he will sell his supplies cheaply.</i> takes up his bunches, he brings coins and redeems. Rebbi Jeremiah said, as much as a person wants Friday afternoon from women braiders<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">They are fully occupied and will react only if they get an extraordinarily cheap buy. R. Jeremiah seems to have no compunctions to go to a women’s hairdresser shop!</i> when he passes by, he brings coins and redeems. Rebbi Yudan bar Gadya<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">He seems to be R. Yudan bar Guria.</i> said, Rebbi Jacob bar Abun left it lying until it got moldy and redeemed it immediately after the Sabbath.
Rebbi Mana had oil which he brought down to Acco<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Where olive oil is more expensive than at the place of production in Galilee.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada said to him, if your oil there at the parapet<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Second Tithe money oil at your house in Galilee.</i> is still there, it is your time that you should redeem it for [there] it is not worth six minas<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of שיתא מני the Rome ms. has מינך “from you”; a corruption.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, one redeems Second Tithe by the offers of three buyers, even if one of them is a Gentile, even if one of them is an owner. Rebbi Jonah asked: Not two Gentiles, not two owners; also not one Gentile and one owner? No, it was said separately<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Only one Gentile, only one owner, but one Gentile and one owner is acceptable. This is also accepted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 12b" href="/Sanhedrin.12b">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 12b</a>, where the owner and his wife are accepted, in Rashi’s interpretation on condition that the wife have her own business and not be dependent on her husband for her upkeep.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the owner says<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">He offers for redemption of his Second Tithe. He has to add a fifth of the amount, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:31" href="/Leviticus.27.31"><i>Lev</i>. 27:31</a>.</i> a tetradrachma and another says a tetradrachma, the owner is preferred since he adds a fifth. The owner says a tetradrachma and someone else says a tetradrachma and an as, the one who offers a tetradrachma and an as is preferred since he adds to the capital<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">By contrast, for redemption of property dedicated to the Temple, the other person would have to offer more than 125% of the owner’s offer before his offer would be considered. The difference between the Second Tithe and Temple dedication is (a) that redemption money of tithe remains in the hand of the owner (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Arakhin 27b" href="/Arakhin.27b">Babli <i>Arakhin</i>27b</a>) and (b) for Second Tithe, the owner can get out of the obligation to pay the additional fifth (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.4.3.1">Mishnah 4</a>).</i>. He who redeems his own Second Tithe adds a fifth, whether it is his own or was given to him as a gift<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of the last clause depends on whether one holds that Second Tithe is the owner’s money, then Second Tithe may be a gift, or whether it is Heaven’s money in the hand of the farmer, when it can be given only as <i>ṭevel</i> but not as tithe.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Is not the fifth of this more than the addition of that one<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The “fifth” is a full drachma, an as is only 1/96 of a tetradrachma.</i>? Rebbi Abin said, there is a difference that he can circumvent and free it from the fifth<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the next Mishnah.</i>.
“A tetradrachma and an as is preferred since he adds to the capital”. Rebbi Jacob bar Idi in the name of Rebbi Simai: One does not add a fifth for any Second Tithe which itself is not worth a <i>peruṭa.</i> Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: One does not add a fifth for any Second Tithe for which the fifth is not worth a <i>peruṭa</i><sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 4:5:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.4.5.2-3">Yerushalmi <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i>4:5,</a> fol. 9d, finds a basis for both rules in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:31" href="/Leviticus.27.31"><i>Lev</i>. 27:31</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Mamai objected: Did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Baba Meẓi‘a,</i> Mishnah 4.6: One cannot force an oath if the defending party has not admitted owing at least a <i>peruṭa</i>; a woman can be preliminarily married only for a gift worth a <i>peruṭa</i>; a person deriving a <i>peruṭa</i>’s worth of benefit from anything dedicated to the Temple has committed larceny; if a find is worth a <i>peruṭa</i> it must be publicized; if somebody robs another of at least a <i>peruṭa</i> and swears falsely, he cannot atone for it unless he makes restitution even if that means a very long trip.</i> “there are five <i>peruṭot</i>”, should one not state “six” according to Rebbi Simai, “Second Tithe which is worth a <i>peruṭa</i>!” And “seven” according to Rebbi Joḥanan, “the fifth of Second Tithe worth a <i>peruṭa</i>.”<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 4:5:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.4.5.2-3">Yerushalmi <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 4:5,</a> fol. 9d, considers this a contradiction to the Mishnah. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 55a" href="/Bava_Metzia.55a">Babli, <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 55a</a>, notes that the Mishnah is restricted to original obligations.</i> In addition, from what Rebbi Yose said in the name of Rebbi Mana bar Tanḥum, Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Real estate cannot be acquired for less than a <i>peruṭa</i><sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">This is a principle accepted everywhere in the Yerushalmi, <i>Eruvin</i>6, fol. 23c; <i>Qiddu</i>š<i>in</i> 1:3, fol. 59d; 1:5, fol 60c. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 13a" href="/Kiddushin.13a">Babli, <i>Qiddušin</i> 13a</a>, it is noted that the statement holds only for acquisition by money but not for barter.</i>. In addition, from the following: If the value of Second Tithe is not known<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">It is unknowable because it is less than a <i>peruṭa</i> and cannot be measured by money’s worth.</i>, it is enough that one say: it and its fifth shall be exchanged for this tetradrachma. Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Crispus, Rebbi Jonah in the name of Rebbi Zeïra: this <i>baraita</i> deals with a tetradrachma of tithe money where it is impossible that it not contain some profane<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">This presupposes the statement of R. Joḥanan in the next paragraph that coins given for tithe money are holy only up to the actual value of the tithe exchanged for that coin. The argument is logical since nobody would offer a tetradrachma for anything valued less than a <i>peruṭa</i>, less than 0.2% of the value of the coin.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, if somebody redeemed dedications<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Gifts for the upkeep of the Temple, to be redeemed by the giver or to be sold by the Temple treasurer.</i> for more than their worth, [the Temple] grabs everything<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The entire sum becomes Temple property.</i>. If somebody redeemed Second Tithe for more than its worth, it does not grab everything. What is the difference between dedications and Second Tithe? Rebbi Immi said, because a person usually adds to his dedications<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Since dedications are voluntary gifts, in contrast to tithe which is an obligation.</i>. Rebbi Zeïra asked before Rebbi Immi: If the person<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Redeeming tithe. If he is asked and declares the entire amount as holy, his word stands. But nobody has to ask.</i> was checked and he said, that was not what I intended? He said to him, when he will be checked. 
Rebbi Jonah asked, that is following him<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">R. Meїr. One can understand the reluctance to give more if the money is Heaven’s money in an obligation imposed. But for R. Jehudah, who holds that tithe money is property of the farmer, why should he care which label is attached to his money since all will be spent for his own benefit?</i> who said that it is not his property. But for him who says that it is his property, what is the difference between dedications and Second Tithe? Rebbi Yose said, the reason has already been explained, because a person usually adds to his dedications.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, if somebody redeemed his dedication without adding a fifth, it is redeemed. If somebody redeemed his Second Tithe without adding a fifth, it is not redeemed. What is the difference between dedications and Second Tithe? Rebbi Hila said, dedications have claimants, Second Tithe has no claimants<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The Temple treasurer has an organization dedicated to collect monies due the Temple; Second Tithe is a matter purely between the farmer and Heaven. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 54a" href="/Bava_Metzia.54a">Babli, <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i>54a</a>, R. Eliezer holds that the fifth never precludes redemption but the Sages hold that the fifth always precludes redemption and R. Joḥanan decides with R. Eliezer for dedications because of the reason given here by R. Hila. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Maaser Sheni (Lieberman) 4:5" href="/Tosefta_Maaser_Sheni_(Lieberman).4.5">Tosephta (4:5</a>), Rebbi holds with R. Eliezer for Second Tithe on the Sabbath but not on weekdays. The argument shows that according to everybody, the position of the Sages only represents a rabbinic institution.</i>. Rebbi Jonah asked, that is following him<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">R. Meїr. One can understand the reluctance to give more if the money is Heaven’s money in an obligation imposed. But for R. Jehudah, who holds that tithe money is property of the farmer, why should he care which label is attached to his money since all will be spent for his own benefit?</i> who said, it is not his property. But for him, who says it is his property, what is the difference between dedications and Second Tithe? Rebbi Yose said, the reason has already been explained, dedications have claimants, Second Tithe has no claimants.
“He who redeems his own Second Tithe adds a fifth, whether it was his own or was given to him as a gift.” Our Mishnah is Rebbi Meїr’s since Rebbi Meїr says, a gift is not like a sale<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ma‘serot</i> 5:1, Note 5. If a gift were a moneyless sale, the produce would not be his and be free from the fifth.</i>. Rebbi Jonah said, it is the opinion of everybody, explain it with produce still <i>ṭevel</i> for tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was his property at the moment the Second Tithe became due, even without the Mishnah we would have known that he has to add the fifth.</i>. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 5:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.5.3.1">Mishnah 5:5</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:23-24" href="/Leviticus.19.23-24"><i>Lev.</i> 19:23–24</a> requires that the fruit of a newly planted tree not be eaten for the first three years. In the fourth year, “all its fruit shall be holy as a praise to the Eternal.” The rules of this holiness are given in the next Chapter. Fruits far from Jerusalem have to be redeemed but, being intrinsically the Eternal’s, they are not subject to heave and tithes and can never be <i>ṭevel.</i></i>: “He who redeems his own fourth-year tree adds a fifth, whether it is his own or was given to him as a gift”? Can you say that this speaks about produce still <i>ṭevel</i> for (Second Tithe? We have stated)<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Missing in Rome ms., probably correctly.</i> “fourth-year tree!” That means the fourth-year tree itself! So here, you will end up saying “tithe”<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Since the wording in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 5:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.5.3.1">Mishnah 5:5</a> speaks about produce to be redeemed, the parallel language in the Mishnah here must also speak about tithe, not <i>ṭevel,</i> and the Mishnah must be R. Meїr’s.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One may circumvent about Second Tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Circumvent the payment of the fifth.</i>. How is this?
