A person may say to his neighbor, his adult son or daughter, his Hebrew male or female slave<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">This rule is purely theoretical since the institution of Hebrew slavery was intrinsically bound to the land distribution by Joshua and automatically disappeared with the destruction of the first Temple, never to be re-instituted. The male Hebrew “slave” is not a slave but a servant indentured for six years (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:18" href="/Deuteronomy.15.18"><i>Deut.</i> 15:18</a>) and remains a legal person. The female Hebrew slave is necessarily a minor and supposed to be married by a member of the master’s family.</i>: Take these coins and redeem this tithe for yourself. But he should not say so to his minor son or daughter or his Canaanite male or female slave<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">They are not Canaanites but originally Gentile slaves or their offspring who became semi-Jewish by circumcision and/or immersion in water. If manumitted, they will automatically become full Jews but as slaves they have no independent legal standing.</i> because their hand is like his hand.<br>If somebody was standing on the threshing floor and no coins were in his hand<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">If the coins were in his hand, he could give them to another person and let him redeem the Second Tithe without paying a fifth.</i>, he says to a friend, this produce is given to you as a gift; then he goes on and says to him, it is redeemed<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Since now the produce is not his own, he does not have to pay the fifth. For what has to be formally given to the neighbor, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 4:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.4.2.1">Note 54</a>.</i> by coins in the house.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Abun said, Rebbi Eleazar and Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina disagree. One said, why may one circumvent it? For “blessing” is written about it<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:24" href="/Deuteronomy.14.24"><i>Deut</i>. 14:24</a>, “because the Eternal, your God, will bless you.” This is taken as authorization to be lenient in several respects.</i>. The other said, why is it redeemed at a cheap rate? For “blessing” is written about it.
How do we hold? If he said to him, go and redeem for me, he is his agent<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The status of the agent is the status of his employer; a fifth is due.</i>; go and redeem for yourself, they are the other’s property<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">A fifth is due for the gift as stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 4:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.4.2.1">Mishnah 3</a>.</i>. But we have to hold that he said to him, redeem for me of your property, or redeem for yourself of my property. We have stated thus: “Redeem for me of your property, or redeem for yourself of my property, he does not add a fifth.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, for any tithe, where neither it nor its redemption money is his, he does not have to add a fifth. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina, the reason of Rebbi Joḥanan (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:31" href="/Leviticus.27.31"><i>Lev.</i> 27:31</a>): “If a person goes to redeem of his own tithes, his own fifth he shall add to it;” both it and its redemption money must be his.
How do we hold? If she<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The female Hebrew slave who when she becomes an adult either is a wife or a free adult; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.4.3.1">Note 75</a>. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 65a" href="/Gittin.65a">Babli (<i>Giṭṭin</i> 65a</a>) restricts the female Hebrew slave to redeeming heave of produce not grown in the soil (in a flower pot without a hole). This answer is very questionable for many reasons.</i> is an adult, she acquired [her freedom] by the signs [of puberty]<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">It is argued (<i>Mekhilta Mišpaṭim</i>3) that the verse (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:7" href="/Exodus.21.7"><i>Ex.</i> 21:7</a>) which gave the father the right to sell his daughter also restricted her servitude to the period in which the father had this right, before she became an adult.</i>; if she is a minor, may a minor acquire? Rebbi Yudan bar Shalom said before Rebbi Yose, explain it following him who said, a minor may give heave<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 1:1:13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.1.1.13"><i>Terumot</i> 1:1, Notes 56–58</a>.</i>. He said to him, even following him who said, a minor may give heave, may a minor acquire? Following the opinion of the rabbis there<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia.</i> it is acceptable since there, they say in the name of Rav Naḥman bar Jacob: One to whom one gives a nut and he throws it away, a pebble and he keeps it, what is found in his hand is as if found on a garbage heap; a nut and he keeps it, a pebble and he throws it away, what is robbed from him is robbed because of communal peace; a nut or a pebble he takes, hides them, and produces them later, what is robbed from him is total robbery. He can acquire for himself but not for others<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 65a" href="/Gittin.65a">Babli, <i>Giṭṭin</i> 65a</a>, the formulation (by Rava, student of Rav Naḥman) is: “There are three stages for minors. If he throws away a pebble and takes a nut, he can acquire for himself but not for others; for school children, their buying is buying and their selling selling, for movables; if they reached the time of vows (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Terumot 1:3" href="/Mishnah_Terumot.1.3"><i>Terumot</i>, Mishnah 1:3, Note 105</a>) their vows and dedications are valid; but to sell inherited real estate {without permission of the court} one must be 20 years old.”</i>. Rav Huna said, just as he can acquire for himself so he can acquire for others. Everybody agrees<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 106b" href="/Bava_Kamma.106b">Babli, <i>Baba Qama</i> 106b</a>, concurs.</i> that his gift is not a gift since it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6" href="/Exodus.22.6"><i>Ex.</i> 22:6</a>): “If a man give.” The gift of a man is a gift, but the gift of a minor is no gift, the words of the Sages. Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, robbing from him is not total robbery unless he grew two pubic hairs. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, that is, to recover from him by a law suit, but to have to bring a sacrifice for [a false] oath only if he grew two pubic hairs<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The minor, or an adult representing him, can successfully prosecute the robber from a minor in court but if in the course of the proceedings the accused swears falsely that he did not take anything, he is not obliged to bring (or, if he has a guilty conscience, he is barred from bringing) a guilt sacrifice since his robbery was forbidden by police law, not biblical law.</i>. But following the rabbis here, Rebbi Yose asked that even for himself he should not be able to acquire since it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6" href="/Exodus.22.6"><i>Ex</i>. 22:6</a>): “To his neighbor”, until he be like his neighbor. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Samuel ben Rav Isaac explained it by the method of small children. As we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.9.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 5:9</a>. The quote here shows that ה and א were both silent.</i>: “For school children, their buying is buying and their selling selling, for movables.” But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.6.1">Mishnah <i>Eruvin</i> 7:6</a>. In order to turn a dead-end street into a private domain for the purpose of carrying on the Sabbath, one has to affix to it a symbolic gate and then all residents of the dead-end street have to contribute to the food for a common meal. It is acceptable that one person designate the food, e. g., a wine barrel, and then appoints somebody to acquire for the other dwellers their part for this Sabbath. The qualifications in that Mishnah are the same as in the Mishnah here, with the same questions about the qualifications of the female Hebrew slave. {Since the devices of participation and <i>eruv</i> are ascribed to King Solomon, the mention of the Hebrew slave can be justified in that case.}</i>: “He cannot make them acquire through his minor son or daughter or his Canaanite male or female slave, because their hand is like his hand.” The rabbis of Caesarea say, here a minor with knowledge<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that a “minor with knowledge” is a minor who reached the time of vows, cf. <i>Tosaphot Sanhedrin</i>68b, <i>s</i>. <i>v</i>. קטן.</i>, there a minor without knowledge.
<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Parallels to this paragraph and the next are in <i>Eruvin</i> 7, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:2-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.2-14"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:3</a>, fol. 60a.</i> There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 8:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.8.3.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 8:4</a>; Babli 98b. If the borrowed animal dies, the borrower is liable to pay. Therefore, it is important to determine the exact time at which the obligation of the borrower starts. This is the moment in which disposal of the cow is transferred from lender to borrower. The Mishnah states that if the borrower asks that the cow be brought to him by the lender’s slave, he becomes liable as soon as the slave goes on his way. But this seems to contradict the Mishnah here; if the slave’s hand (in the legal sense) is his owner’s then the cow should not leave the lender’s domain until she is handed over to the borrower since nobody doubts that if the owner himself drives the cow to the borrower, the latter’s responsibility starts only at the moment of delivery, not during the trip.</i>: “If somebody borrows a cow and [the lender] sent her to him through his son, his slave, or his agent.” Does this not say that a slave is able to transfer rights from his master to another person? Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 99a" href="/Bava_Metzia.99a">Babli, <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 99a</a>, the position of R. Eleazar is Samuel’s, that of R. Joḥanan is Rav’s.</i> said, explain it about a Hebrew slave. Rebbi Joḥanan said, you can even explain it for a Canaanite slave, it he told him, open the gate for her and she will go by herself, as we have stated: If he led her, drew her, called her<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli: Hit her with a stick.</i> and she followed him, he is required to pay as a borrower.
Rebbi Zeïra understood it from here: “But he cannot make them acquire through his minor son or daughter or his Canaanite male or female slave, because their hand is not like his hand.” Does this not imply that a slave is not able to transfer rights from his master to another person? Explain it following Rebbi Meïr since Rebbi Meïr makes the hand of the slave the hand of his master<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Everywhere in the Mishnah. The <i>baraita</i> denies legal standing to minors and slaves.</i>. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.6.1">Mishnah <i>Eruvin</i> 7:6</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 4:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.4.3.4">Note 89</a>. In the list of people empowered, the wife is noted together with the adult children since her husband cannot make an <i>eruv</i> for her without her consent.</i> “his wife”? Rebbi Meïr holds that the hand of the wife is the hand of her husband! Rebbi Ḥananiah said in the name of Rebbi Phineas, explain it following the Tanna who stated<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Maaser Sheni (Lieberman) 4:7" href="/Tosefta_Maaser_Sheni_(Lieberman).4.7">Tosephta 4:7</a>. In the Babli, Rava restricts the statement of R. Simeon ben Eleazar to the case that the wife inherited Second Tithe. Since R. Meïr holds that Second Tithe is Heaven’s money, the Second Tithe does not become part of the estate. If the wife now redeems the tithe with household money, the money is the husband’s but the tithe is not; therefore, no fifth is due. The Babli rejects the conclusion of the Yerushalmi here.</i>: “His wife cannot redeem Second Tithe for him. Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar says in the name of Rebbi Meïr, his wife can redeem Second Tithe for him.” For that Tanna, Rebbi Meïr makes the hand of the slave the hand of his master but not the hand of the wife the hand of her husband!
In earlier times, they were doing so with coins<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">The farmer gave coins to a third party as a gift that the recipient should redeem his Second Tithe.</i>. They took them and fled<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Maaser Sheni (Lieberman) 4:3" href="/Tosefta_Maaser_Sheni_(Lieberman).4.3">Tosephta (4:3</a>), R. Joshua ben Qorḥa reports that the practice was discontinued since many people did not give the coins as gifts.</i>. They instituted that one does it with produce<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">The procedure explained in the Mishnah.</i>. Nevertheless, they took and ate it. They instituted that he should give him the right to one in ten on the ground<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <i>Peah</i> 3:8, a square inch of ground can be given to the recipient and the ownership of the produce transferred with it. In that case, ownership can be transferred without the produce actually coming into the hands of the second person who, therefore, cannot eat it. This sentence is not in the Tosephta.</i>.
Rebbi Inaya bar Sisai<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">He is R. Ḥanina bar Sisai; cf. <i>Berakhot</i> p. 493.</i> came to Rebbi Jonah and said to him, shall I redeem for you with that tetradrachma<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">With R. Jonah’s coin, in the style of earlier times, as given in the previous <i>baraita.</i></i>? He said, if you want to take it, take it. Then he took it back from him. Rebbi Jonah said, I had judged his opinion that if I took it back he would not say anything; therefore I gave it to him<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">If there was no danger that the other person would run away with the money, the earlier practice is preferable to that of the Mishnah.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If [somebody] took from [another] tithe in the value of a tetradrachma<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">A takes from B Second Tithe produce with the understanding that he will pay B one tetradrachma which will become tithe money and which B will be obliged to take to Jerusalem and spend there. As far as the civil contract is concerned, the act of taking concludes the contract and the monetary obligation is fixed. But the verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:25" href="/Deuteronomy.14.25"><i>Deut</i>. 14:25</a> decrees that redemption of Second Tithe is the giving of money. Therefore, the redemption is carried out only at the moment of payment and an additional tetradrachma from the buyer should become tithe money for the buyer, not the seller. Then the Second Tithe becomes profane in the hand of the buyer but not before. (Explanation of כסף משנה to Maimonides, <i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i>8:7.)</i> and did not pay to redeem it until it became worth two, he gives him a tetradrachma, gains a tetradrachma, and the Second Tithe is his. If [somebody] took from [another] tithe in the value of two and did not pay to redeem it until it became worth one tetradrachma, he gives him a tetradrachma of profane money and a tetradrachma of his Second Tithe [money]<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">The situation is essentially the same as before but, since it was stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 4:2:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.4.2.2-7">Halakhah 3</a> that Second Tithe cannot be redeemed for more than its market value, only one tetradrachma can be tithe money but naturally he has to fulfill his contract and pay two tetradrachmas. Therefore, the buyer must give at least one tetradrachma of profane coin which becomes tithe money; the other coin he may pay either in profane money and it remains profane or in tithe money which remains tithe money and has to be taken to Jerusalem.</i>. If he is a vulgar, he gives him from his <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">This reading is the Maimonides tradition (the vocalization follows the Yemenite tradition) and the first explanation of R. Simson. Since tithe money may not be delivered to a vulgar, the second coin due has to be profane or from money reserved from <i>demay</i> tithe. The second explanation of R. Simson reads מִדָּמָיו (with the corrector of the ms.) “from his money” and requires that the vulgar be paid 100% in profane money where only one coin becomes tithe money.</i>.<br>If somebody redeemed Second Tithe and did not give it a name<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">He thought to redeem the tithe but did not voice his intention.</i>, Rebbi Yose says it is sufficient, Rebbi Jehudah says he has to be explicit. If a man was talking with a woman about her bill of divorce or her preliminary marriage and gave her [the valuable for] preliminary marriage and did not spell it out<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">The usual interpretation is that he gave the valuable and said, take this to be married, but not: take this to be married <i>to me</i>. While in general it is required that the person should be named, if it is clear from the context, one may dispense with it following R. Yose.</i>, Rebbi Yose says it is sufficient, Rebbi Jehudah says he has to be explicit.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> The Mishnah does not follow Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Maaser Sheni (Lieberman) 4:14" href="/Tosefta_Maaser_Sheni_(Lieberman).4.14">Tosephta, 4:14</a>, this is the majority opinion: “The redemption of Second Tithe is its sale. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel and R. Ismael ben R. Joḥanan ben Beroqa say, also for a dedicated thing, its being taken up is its redemption.” For these authors, the matter of payment is purely an affair of civil, not religious, law between the parties.</i>: “Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, always the taking up of Second Tithe is its redemption.”
Rebbi Assi in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: One does not add a fifth to the second tetradrachma<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">The one which remains in the hand of the buyer in the first case.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">This name tradition, found in both mss., is impossible since R. Eleazar, the originator of the rule in question, died before R. Yose’s time. “Staring” is always a sign of disapproval.</i> stared at him. He said to him, why do you stare at me? Rebbi Hila also agrees with it, following what Rebbi Joḥanan said, for any tithe, where not it and its redemption money is his<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">While in this case the Second Tithe was acquired by being handed over by the seller to the buyer, this acquisition is not counted for the Second Tithe which may not be sold except for redemption. It follows that by biblical standards, the tithe at the moment of its redemption by A was still B’s tithe, even if it was not B’s property.</i>, he does not add a fifth.
How must he be explicit? “This is your bill of divorce, or this is for your preliminary marriage,” and here: “This is for redemption of Second Tithe.”
Rebbi Zeïra, Ḥiyya bar Abun, Abba bar Taḥlifa, in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia, when do they differ? If they thought about other things but if they still were on the same subject, it is a bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 6a" href="/Kiddushin.6a">Babli, <i>Qiddušin</i> 6a</a>, the statement is in the names of Samuel and R. Hoshaia.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">The parallel to this and the next paragraph is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:1:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.1.2-9"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 6:1,</a> fol. 47d.</i> Rebbi Ḥaggai asked before Rebbi Yose: Rebbi follows Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Nathan Rebbi Jehudah, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.1.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 6:1</a>, referring to the delivery of a bill of divorce if husband and wife are at different places. Since the verse decrees (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>): “He shall write her a bill of divorce and give it into her hand,” a divorce is not valid unless the bill of divorce is written on the order of the husband and delivered into the hand of the wife. If she appoints an agent, all depends on the instruction of the agent. If he is appointed to receive the bill in her stead, she is divorced the moment the agent receives it. If he is appointed to bring her the bill, she is divorced only when the bill is delivered into her hand. In the second case, it is the majority opinion that the husband may change his mind and annul the divorce anytime the bill has not yet been delivered. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 4:6:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.4.6.6"><i>Peah</i> 4. Note 119</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 4:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.4.4.2"><i>Demay</i> 4, Note 83</a>.<br>In the case mentioned here, the husband refuses to recognize the commission of the agent to receive the bill but appoints him his own agent to deliver the bill. Since the wife’s commission was explicitly refused, the agent is now exclusively the husband’s agent; there are no legal complications. The complications arise if either the agent does not exactly repeat his instructions to the husband or if the husband appoints him his agent without explicitly repudiating the wife’s commission.</i>: “I cannot accept that you accept it for her, but here you have it and give it to her, if he wants to take it back, he may take it back.” The Mishnah is Rebbi’s, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 6:1" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).6.1">Tosephta <i>Giṭṭin</i> 6:1</a>. The opinion ascribed in the Yerushalmi to R. Nathan is reported in the Tosephta as that of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel. But the <i>baraita</i> quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 63a" href="/Gittin.63a">Babli, <i>Giṭṭin</i> 63a</a>, is the same as the text in the Yerushalmi. The Babli also notes that <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.1.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 6:1</a> states Rebbi’s opinion.</i>: “If she said, bring me my bill of divorce, but he said to him, your wife said, accept my bill of divorce for me. ‘Bring it to her, give it to her, acquire it for her, receive it for her’, if he wants to take it back, he may not take it back, the words of Rebbi. Rebbi Nathan says, ‘bring it to her, give it to her,’ if he wants to take it back, he may take it back; ‘acquire it for her, receive it for her’, if he wants to take it back, he may not take it back.” Rebbi says, in any of these cases he may not take it back except if he says, “I cannot accept that you accept it for her, but give it to her.” It is difficult for Rebbi, “here you have it from my word”, if he wants to take it back, he may not take it back. It is difficult for Rebbi Nathan, “here you have it from her word”, if he wants to take it back, he may not take it back<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">The last two sentences are R. Ḥaggai’s argument: If he says, “here you have it from my word,” is that not invalidating the wife’s commission? In that case, “give it to her” is also invalidating the wife’s commission and he should be able to change his mind. For R. Nathan, the argument is the opposite: “bring it to her, give it to her,” can be read as accepting the wife’s commission; he should not be able to change his mind.</i>. Rebbi Ḥuna<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 63b" href="/Gittin.63b">Babli, <i>Giṭṭin</i> 63b</a>, this is the opinion of Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Huna in the name of Rav.</i> said, he becomes his and her agent<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli explains that in this case, it is doubtful which commission the agent executes and, therefore, whether his action is legal.</i>. Assi<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of the parallel in <i>Giṭṭin</i>.</i> said, everywhere we have stated “his and her agent”, she is divorced and not divorced<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">She cannot remarry without a second bill of divorce and, if she is childless and the husband dies before executing the second bill, she cannot marry her brother-in-law.</i>. He<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">This is now R. Yose’s (the Amora) response to R. Ḥaggai, that the situation described in the Mishnah here is not comparable to that in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:1:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.1.2-9"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 6:1</a>.</i> said to him, what do you have in your hand, did not Rebbi Zeïra, Ḥiyya bar Abun, Abba bar Taḥlifa, say in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia, when do they differ? If they thought about other things but if they still were at the same theme, it is a bill of divorce.
Rebbi Ezra asked before Rebbi Mana: Is it the same for a gift<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Are the rules of agency for receiving a gift the same as for receiving a bill of divorce.</i>? Can a person appoint an agent for something that is not his? He said to him, there the Torah gave her the right to a bill of divorce and she appoints an agent to receive what rightfully is hers<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">The husband cannot divorce her without handing her a bill of divorce.</i>. Can you say in regard to a gift that a person can appoint an agent for something that is not his? <sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the Halakhah, the text is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 10:4:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.10.4.6"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 10:9, Notes 133–134</a>.</i> In addition, from what Rebbi Yose, Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan said, if somebody said to give a gift to another and he wants to take it back, he may take it back. Rebbi Yose stood near to Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi and said to him, is that a just “yes”? They said, at the moment he said it, it was a just yes.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who put aside an <i>as</i> and ate for half of its worth, then went to another place where it is worth a <i>dupondius</i>, has to eat another as for it<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">He put aside a profane <i>as</i> to redeem Second Tithe and ate from his redeemed Second Tithe but it was worth only half an <i>as</i>, then half of the <i>as</i> is still profane. If now he goes to a place where his copper coin is worth a local <i>dupondius</i>, or 2 <i>as</i>, then half the coin there is worth a full <i>as</i> and he has to eat another <i>as</i>’s worth of Second Tithe since it was stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 4:2:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.4.2.2-7">Halakhah 4:3</a> that Second Tithe cannot be redeemed for more than its wholesale value. (Explanation of Maimonides’s first version, R. Simson, and R. Isaac Simponti.)</i>. He who put aside a <i>dupondius</i> and ate for half of its worth, then went to another place where it would be worth an <i>as</i>, has to eat another half<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">By the same argument as before, he has to redeem Second Tithe in the worth of half the coin, which now is half an <i>as</i>.</i>. He who put aside an <i>as</i> of Second Tithe eats for it 11 <i>as</i> and 1/100 of an <i>as</i><sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Maimonides reads אוֹכֵל עָלָיו אַחַד עָשָׂר בְּאִיסָּר “he eats 11/10 of an <i>as</i>.” This is not the reading of the Leyden ms. of the Yerushalmi, the Mishnah in the Babli ms. Munich, and the Cambridge and Kaufmann mss. of the Mishnah. Therefore, it seems that one has to explain the Mishnah following R. Simson and R. Isaac Simponti, that this part of the Mishnah speaks about tithe money which is spent in Jerusalem. The produce is bought from a vulgar who was seen to have separated (unprepared, therefore pure) Second Tithe but who then put the Second Tithe back into his profane produce since he is in Jerusalem. If the fellow then buys from him, nine parts are presumed to be profane and one Second Tithe. Since 1/9 = .1̄, an infinite decimal, in order to turn the <i>as</i> into profane coin, he has to buy for 10/9 <i>as</i>. This means that “11 <i>as</i> and 1/100 of an <i>as</i>” has to be read as “11 <i>as</i> and 11 times 1/100 of an <i>as</i>”.</i>; the House of Shammai say, 10 in total<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">The House of Shammai always, and the House of Hillel in the case of <i>demay</i>, do not believe that the vulgar separated First Tithe. Therefore, he has separately to give 1/10 as First Tithe.</i>, but the House of Hillel say, if it is certain 11<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">They mean, 11.1̄.</i> and for <i>demay</i> 10.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Ḥiyya stated, two <i>as</i> equal a <i>dupondius</i><sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">In the military anarchy and later, <i>as</i> and <i>dupondius</i> had long vanished from circulation and their relationship was no longer generally known.</i>. Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Who lived after Diocletian’s currency reform.</i> said, the Mishnah says so: “He who put aside an <i>as</i> and ate for half its worth, went to another place where the coin is worth a <i>dupondius</i>, has to eat another <i>as</i> for it.”
Samuel says, you cannot state that; when he takes one in ten, he has to take one in a hundred. When he takes one in a hundred he has to take one in a thousand, when he takes one in a thousand he has to take one in ten thousand<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">He refers to the statement of the House of Hillel who qote only 11 instead of 11.11111 … The same argument and a similar text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 7:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.7.6.2"><i>Demay</i> 7:8, Note 104</a>.</i>.
The House of Shammai<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">It is possible that this is an error for “The House of Hillel”. Since both mss. have the same reading, the error must already have been in the common <i>Vorlage</i>. It is more likely that this is a <i>baraita</i> which disagrees with the Mishnah.</i> say, if it is certain 11<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">They mean, 11.1̄.</i> and for <i>demay</i> 10. Bar Qappara stated, both for <i>demay</i> and for certain 11. Rebbi Yudan ben Rebbi Shalom taught what Bar Qappara had stated. The House of Shammai follows Rebbi Eliezer, <sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the paragraph, the text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 4:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.4.3.4"><i>Demay</i> 4:5, Notes 60–62</a>. Since the text there reads: “The <i>baraita</i> follows R. Eliezer,” one has to accept that the text discussed here is a <i>baraita</i>.</i> since Rebbi Eliezer said, he who is trustworthy for the Second is trustworthy for the First. Rebbi Yose said, everybody agrees, they made it like additions to First Fruits. Just as additions to First Fruits are eaten in purity and are free from <i>demay</i>, so this is eaten as Second Tithe and is free from the First. Rebbi Ḥananiah confirmed in the name of Rebbi Issi: it is Rebbi Eliezer’s.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> All found coins<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">The Maimonides autograph Mishnah and the Cambridge codex of the Galilean Mishnah add: “in Jerusalem”. But in Jerusalem during the holidays, all coins found are considered tithe money, and the rest of the year coins found in the animal market are presumed to be tithe money since the main use of tithe money is for well-being sacrifices; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.7.2.1">Mishnah <i>Šeqalim</i>7:2</a>. Therefore, the text here is preferable and the meaning is that coins found anywhere, except in Jerusalem in the few instances enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 7:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.7.2.2"><i>Šeqalim</i> 7:2</a>, are to be considered profane.</i> are profane, even gold denars with silver or change<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Here one could argue that silver and change are the tithe exchange for the gold coin which was the original tithe money.</i>. If one found with them a potsherd reading “tithe”, it is tithe.<br>If one finds a vessel inscribed “sacrifice”, Rebbi Jehudah said, if it was pottery it is profane and its contents sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">A clay pot is too cheap to have been dedicated to the Temple. Therefore, only its contents are dedicated. The Sages do not oppose this statement.</i>, but if it was metal it is sacrifice and its contents profane<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody agrees that metal vessels are valuable enough to be dedicated. Therefore, the vessel has to be considered Temple property (it cannot be a sacrifice). R, Jehudah holds that without further evidence, the contents of the vessel have to be considered profane, but the anonymous Sages hold that nobody uses a dedicated vessel to store profane material.</i>. They said to him, people do not put profane in sacrifice [vessels].<br>If one finds a vessel inscribed ק it means sacrifice, מ tithe, ד <i>demay</i>, ט <i>ṭevel</i>, ת heave since in times of danger one wrote ת for heave<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">This proves the status of anything in the vessel. Again “sacrifice” means “dedicated to the Temple”.</i>. Rebbi Yose says, all these are people’s names<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Everything is profane.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, even if he found an amphora full of produce and inscribed “heave”, these are profane since I say last year it was full of tithe produce but he emptied it.<br>If somebody said to his son, “Second Tithe is in that corner” but he found it in another corner, that is profane<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">We assume the tithe was removed and what was found in another corner is other produce. Since 100 denar (a talent) are a part of 200, one assumes that 100 was taken and 100 remains. On the other hand, 100 cannot grow to 200; 100 must be new money.</i>. “There is a talent” but he found 200, the remainder is profane. “200” and he found 100, all is tithe.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> That you should not say, since people do not usually act like this, one has to say “profane<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">The second part of Mishnah 9; it is not usual that people use an ostracon in a money bag.</i>”.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Maaser Sheni (Lieberman) 5:1" href="/Tosefta_Maaser_Sheni_(Lieberman).5.1">Tosephta 5:1</a>: If one found a vessel inscribed <i>alef dalet reš tav</i>, it is heave. <i>Yod mem</i> is tithe. <i>Pe šin</i> is Second. But the Sages say, all of them are people’s names.</i>: <i>Alef dalet ḥet ṭet mem tav</i> heave. א “first”<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">Heave is called “beginning” in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:12" href="/Numbers.18.12"><i>Num</i>. 18:12</a>.</i>. ד “<i>demay</i>”. ח “its fat”<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">An expression for heave in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:12" href="/Numbers.18.12"><i>Num</i>. 18:12</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:29" href="/Numbers.18.29">29</a>.</i>. ט “its best”. ר “beginning”<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">Heave is called “beginning” in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:12" href="/Numbers.18.12"><i>Num</i>. 18:12</a>.</i>. ת “heave”. ב ש “Second Tithe”<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Either ב for 2<sup class="footnote-marker">nd</sup> or ש for שני.</i>, פ ש “Second Tithe”<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">פ for פדיון or פורקן “redemption”. In that case, the vessel should contain coins of the redemption money (or produce in Jerusalem).</i>. י מ<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">י, numerical value 10, stands for 10%.</i> “tithe”. Tithe for the City<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">In Talmudic texts, “City” stands for Jerusalem, not Byzantion.</i>, redemption. Those on the name of Yose, on the name of Simeon, to take it up to eat in Jerusalem, are profane; I am saying contributions<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">Greek συμβολή.</i> they arranged among themselves.
So far new ones<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">Containers with markings on them.</i>. Old ones, I am saying yesterday it was full of heave but he empties it, like this: Rebbi Jonah and Rebbi Yose were partners in a cellar of (wine)-barrels. When Rebbi Jonah died, Rebbi Mana<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jonah’s son.</i> said to Rebbi Yose: Any barrel on which is written “Rebbi Jonah” is mine. He said to him, yesteryear is was yours, this year it is mine. But if he found that on the lid was written “tithe”, it is tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">Since the lid is easily exchanged, he would not have reused one that said “tithe”.</i>.
Were these exchanged<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of Mishnah 12. Why do we assume that what was found was what was deposited?</i>? We have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.1.5.1">Mishnah <i>Beẓah</i> 1:4</a>. From here to the end of the paragraph, the text is found in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 1:5:4-6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.1.5.4-6.3"><i>Beẓah</i> 1:5,</a> fol. 60c.<br>It is a rule that on holidays, only food may be prepared that was designated as holiday food before the start of the day; cf. <i>Ševiʻit</i> 9:1, Note 24. The Mishnah deals with pigeon chicks in one’s dovecot which cannot yet fly. The problem is that he designated some of these chicks to be taken the next day to be slaughtered and cooked and now on the holiday he finds others.</i>: “If he prepared black ones and found white, white and found black, two and found three, they are forbidden, three and found two they are permitted.” Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Assi, this<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">The last clause, that 2 from 3 is permitted.</i> is Rebbi’s, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Maaser Sheni (Lieberman) 5:7" href="/Tosefta_Maaser_Sheni_(Lieberman).5.7">Tosephta 5:7</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 10b" href="/Beitzah.10b">Babli <i>Beẓah</i>10b</a>, in slightly changed wording.</i>: “Two hundred and he found a talent, a talent was taken, the word of Rebbi, but the Sages say, it is profane.” He turned around and said, this is everybody’s opinion since pigeon chicks usually start to fly<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 10b" href="/Beitzah.10b">Babli, <i>Beẓah</i> 10b</a>, this opinion is attributed to R. Joḥanan and R. Eleazar and is the final statement. The next <i>baraita</i> is not in the Babli sources.</i>. But did not Rebbi Ḥalaphta ben Shaul state: The same rule applies to pigeon chicks and to eggs<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">If he designated eggs in his chicken coop without taking them before the holiday.</i>. Therefore, it is Rebbi’s. There<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">To show that there is comparison between the cases in <i>Beẓah</i> and here. The text in <i>Beẓah</i> reads: “there, his father put it there, he found it.”</i>, he put it there and he found, here, his father put it there, did his father find it? Rebbi Abun bar Cohen said before Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Aḥa: Rebbi Abba bar Zavda taught according to Rebbi for Second Tithe.
<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">In the Rome ms.: If somebody was worried about tithe monies. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Maaser Sheni (Lieberman) 5:6" href="/Tosefta_Maaser_Sheni_(Lieberman).5.6">Tosephta, 5:6</a>, the text is: Somebody was worried about his father’s Second Tithe; the dream spirit appeared to him and said, they are so and so much and at place X. This happened in a case; they found monies there and asked the rabbis who said, they are profane for dreams do not improve or detract. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 30a" href="/Sanhedrin.30a">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 30a</a>: If somebody was worried about the monies his father left him, the dream spirit appeared to him and said, they are so and so much, at place X, and are of Second Tithe. This case came before the rabbis who said, dreams do not improve or detract.</i> Somebody was worried about his father’s monies; it appeared to him in a dream that they were so and so much and at place X. This case came before the rabbis who said, dreams do not improve or detract. Rebbi Jonah asked: He is worried and you say so? Rebbi Yose said, is it not reasonable that it does not apply to somebody who is not worried but sees; but here, what the person is he sees. Rebbi Avin said, whoever wants to act, should act following Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">Dreams are of importance if they are unconnected to the daily activities of the dreamer. A similar statement in the name of the early R. Jonathan is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 55b" href="/Berakhot.55b">Babli <i>Berakhot</i> 55b</a>. This is the introduction to the next section, dealing with the importance of dreams and their interpretation. The fact that all interpretations are given in Aramaic seems to indicate that only for scholars are dreams unimportant.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">The main parallel of this and the following paragraphs is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 1:15" href="/Ecclesiastes.1.15"><i>Midrash Qohelet Rabbati</i> 1(15</a>) ff.; the Babylonian parallels are in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 55b-57b" href="/Berakhot.55b-57b">Babli <i>Berakhot</i>55b–57b</a>. This particular paragraph is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 1:18" href="/Ecclesiastes.1.18"><i>Qohelet Rabbati</i> 1(18</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 56b" href="/Berakhot.56b"><i>Berakhot</i> 56b</a>.</i> A man came before Rebbi Yose ben Ḥalaphta and said to him, I saw in my dream that it was said to me, go to Cappadocia and you will find your father’s property. He asked him, did the father of this man ever go to Cappadocia? He said to him, no. He said to him, go and count 20 rows in your house and you will find your father’s property, κάππα δοκία<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">“κ (<i>kappa</i>, the Alexandrian notation for 20) beams”.</i>.
A person came to Rebbi Yose ben Ḥalaphta and said to him, I saw in my dream that I was wearing a crown of olives. He said to him, you will rise to greatness in the future. Another person came and said to him, I saw in my dream that I was wearing a crown of olives. He said to him, you will be whipped. He said to him, to the other man you said you will rise to greatness and to me you say you will be whipped? He said to him, that other was at blossoming, you at hitting [time]<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">The Rome text is: בוצינא ואת באבטיחיא that other was “at light, but you among water melons.” This makes no sense. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 1:16" href="/Ecclesiastes.1.16"><i>Qohelet Rabbati</i> 1(16</a>), reads: דין חמא בשעת נצבא ודין בשעת חבטה “That one saw at the time of blossoming and this at the time of hitting.” The time the tree is shaken and its branches hit is harvest time. This story has no Babylonian equivalent.</i>.
A person came before Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Qohelet Rabbati</i> and Babli <i>Berakhot</i>, the author is always R. Ismael.</i> and said to him, I saw in my dream that I was watering an olive tree with oil. He said to him, the spirit of that person should be blown away, he made love to his mother. A person came before Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose and said to him, I saw in my dream that my eye kissed its companion. He said to him, the spirit of that person should be blown away, he made love to his sister<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Qohelet Rabbati</i>, one eye swallowed the other and the interpretation was that his children slept with one another. In the Babli, this and the preceding paragraph speak of the same person.</i>. A person came before Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose and said to him, I saw in my dream that I had three eyes. He said to him, you are an oven maker; your two eyes and the opening of the oven<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Qohelet Rabbati</i>, a baker with the glowing opening of his oven.</i>. A person came before Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose and said to him, I saw in my dream that I had four ears. He said to him, you are filling [amphoras]; your two ears and the two ears of the amphora. A person came before Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose and said to him, I saw in my dream that people got out of my way. He said to him, you are carrying thorns and everybody gets out of your way<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Qohelet Rabbati</i>, this and the preceding paragraph are amalgamated to an unintelligible whole.</i>. A person came before Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose and said to him, I saw in my dream that I was wearing a wooden notebinder with twelve tablets. He said to him, the stone bench of this person has twelve tatters<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Qohelet Rabbati</i>, the cover of his donkey has 12 tears.</i>. A person came before Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose and said to him, I saw in my dream that I swallowed a star. He said to him, the spirit of that person should be blown away, he killed a Jew as it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 24:17" href="/Numbers.24.17"><i>Num.</i> 24:17</a>): “A star went out from Jacob.<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Qohelet Rabbati</i>, the proof is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 15:5" href="/Genesis.15.5"><i>Gen.</i> 15:5</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 56b" href="/Berakhot.56b">Babli, <i>Berakhot</i>56b</a>, he is accused of selling a Jew into slavery.</i>” A person came before Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose and said to him, I saw in my dream the vineyard of this man grow bitter lettuce. He said to him, all your wine will be sour and you will bring bitter lettuce and dip it in the cups<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Qohelet Rabbati</i>, all people will come to buy his vinegar to pickle the bitter lettuce.</i>.
A person came to Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose and said to him, I saw in my dream that it was said to me, throw your fingers, descend. He said to him, give me my fee and I shall tell you. He said to him, I saw in my dream that it was said to me, blow with your mouth. He said to him, give me my fee and I shall tell you. He said to him, I saw in my dream that it was said to me, straighten your fingers upwards. He said to him, did I not say to you, give me my fee and I shall tell you! When it was said to you [first], [the rain] dripped down on your wheat. When it was said to you next, they became swollen. When it was said to you next, they sprouted<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 1:15" href="/Ecclesiastes.1.15"><i>Qohelet Rabbati</i> 1(15</a>), the same story is much more wordy, finding R. Ismael in competition with a Samaritan interpreter of dreams. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 56" href="/Berakhot.56">Babli, <i>Berakhot</i> 56a/b</a>, there is a story about a Babylonian interpreter who took money and came to a bad end.</i>.
A Samaritan said, I shall make fun of this elder of the Jews. He went to him and said, I saw in my dream four cedars, four sycamores, a stand, the skin of a cow<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">This is then interpreted as a pun, reading תורתא either as feminine of תור “ox, cattle”, Hebrew שוֹר, or of תורא “row”, Hebrew שוּר.</i>, and this man sits and steps on it. He said to him, the spirit of that person should be blown away, this is no dream! Nevertheless, you should not go away empty-handed. The four cedars are the four sides of his bed, the four sycomores the four legs of his bed, the stand with a skin, the basis for straw, and the rows the fingers. And this man sits and steps on it: this man lies on them, he cannot live and cannot die. And that happened to him<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">The text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 1:16" href="/Ecclesiastes.1.16"><i>Qohelet Rabbati</i> 1(16</a>) is quite different.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">The original story is in <i>Qohelet Rabbati</i> 1(19); a text parallel to the Yerushalmi in <i>Gen. rabba</i> 89(10).</i> A woman came before Rebbi Eliezer. She said to him, I saw in my dream the pillar<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">The unexplained word תיניתא is translated following the text in <i>Gen. rabba</i> which has שוריתא.</i> of the house breaking. He said, you will give birth to a male child. She went and gave birth to a male. After some time she came and asked for him. His students said, he is not here. They asked her, what do you want from him? She said to them, this woman<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., I.</i> saw in my dream that the pillar of the house was breaking. They said to her, you will give birth to a male child<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">The version here is telescoped from the text in <i>Qohelet Rabbati.</i> There, R. Eliezer himself told her twice that she would have a son; the students made the bad interpretation only the third time.</i> and the husband of that woman<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., you.</i> dies. When Rebbi Eliezer came, they told him what happened. He said to them, you killed a person for the dream goes only after its interpretation as it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 41:13" href="/Genesis.41.13"><i>Gen.</i> 41:13</a>): “It was just as he had interpreted it for us, so it happened.”
Rebbi Joḥanan said, all dreams follow their interpretations except for wine. There is one who drinks and it is good for him and one who drinks and it is bad for him. A scholar drinks and it is good for him, a vulgar drinks and it is bad for him<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">This is the end of the story of R. Eliezer in both <i>Qohelet Rabbati</i> 1(19) and <i>Gen. rabba</i> 89(10). In the latter text, R. Abbahu dissents and states that dreams do not improve or detract.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 1:17" href="/Ecclesiastes.1.17"><i>Qohelet Rabbati</i> 1(17</a>), the author is R. Joḥanan. The last story, of the student, is told in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 56b" href="/Berakhot.56b">Babli, <i>Berakhot</i> 56b</a>, of Bar Qappara and Rebbi.</i> A person came to Rebbi Aqiba and said to him, I saw in my dream my foot shrinking. He said to him, holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">A holiday of pilgrimage, which in biblical Hebrew is called “foot”.</i> is coming but you will not eat read meat<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">As <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 141 explains, the biblical command of rejoicing on a holiday can only be fulfilled by the consumption of meat from animals acceptable as holiday sacrifices.</i>. Another person came and said, I saw in my dream my foot swelling. He said to him, holiday is coming and you will have plenty of red meat. A student of Rebbi Aqiba was sitting making a queer face. He said to him, why is that? He said to him, I saw in a dream three hard things: In Adar you will die, Nisan you will not see, and what you will sow you will not collect. He said to him, all three are good. In the glory<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">A pun of אדר and הדר is possible only if ה has lost all sound.</i> of the Torah you will be lifted, wonders you will not see<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Qohelet Rabbati</i> and Babli <i>Berakhot,</i> “you will not undergo temptations”.</i>, and what you will sow you will not collect, what you will have born to you you will not bury.

Chapter 5



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One marks a fourth-year vineyard with lumps of earth, one of ‘<i>orlah</i> with potsherds<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The fruits of newly planted trees, including vines, are forbidden for all use during the first three years; this is called ‘<i>orlah.</i> In the fourth year, the fruits are (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:24" href="/Leviticus.19.24"><i>Lev.</i> 19:24</a>) “holy exultation for the Eternal”, they have to be eaten under the rules of Second Tithe in Jerusalem or be redeemed and the redemption money treated under the rules of tithe money.<br>The anonymous Tanna requires that such a vineyard be marked so that people would not inadvertently take from its fruits and sin. For him, one marks the fourth-year vineyard with temporary markings but ‘<i>orlah</i>, which extends over a few rainy seasons, with markers that remain after the rains. Rabban Gamliel holds that we are not responsible for thieves, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 3:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.3.5.2"><i>Demay</i> 3:5, Note 135</a>; therefore, it is only necessary to mark forbidden orchards or vineyards in the Sabbatical where everybody has the right to take the fruits.</i>; graves with whitewash which is thinned and poured<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Single graves in an agricultural area must be made easily recognizable so that Cohanim can avoid them.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Gamliel said, when has this been said? In a Sabbatical year. But discreet religious persons set aside some coins and say, what is taken from here is exchanged for these coins<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">If the fourth year of the vineyard is a Sabbatical, the obligation of redemption is not lifted. In that year, scrupulous followers of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel nevertheless take precautions lest people sin inadvertently. In that case, the vineyard does not have to be marked.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Zenon asked Rebbi: How should we state, fourth-year vineyard or fourth-year orchard? He said to them, go out and ask Rebbi Isaac the Elder<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">A Tanna of the fourth generation, companion of R. Nathan, older than Rebbi.</i> with whom I checked the entire Mishnah. He said to them, the first ones fourth-year vineyard, the second ones fourth-year orchard. Rebbi Zeïra complained about the old people from the time of Rebbi Isaac the Elder, that they did not check all Mishnaiot with him.
It was stated: One marks a fourth-year vineyard with lumps of earth because it is temporary, one of ‘<i>orlah</i> with potsherds because it has to last longer; graves with whitewash because it has to last still longer. Rebbi Ḥalaphta ben Shaul stated: If [the ‘<i>orlah</i> trees] were isolated, one hangs amphora handles on them.
Rebbi Zeïra asked, why do we not enumerate all for what they are: As we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Maaser Sheni (Lieberman) 5:13" href="/Tosefta_Maaser_Sheni_(Lieberman).5.13">Tosephta 5:13</a>.</i>: “A dedicated tree one colors with vermilion, houses of idol worship one blackens with charcoal, a leprous house one indicates by burned ashes, the place of a slain person by blood, the place where the neck of a calf was broken<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">To atone for an unsolved murder case, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:1-9" href="/Deuteronomy.21.1-9"><i>Deut</i>. 21:1–9</a>. The place is permanently forbidden for agricultural use, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:4" href="/Deuteronomy.21.4">v. 4</a>.</i> by a stone enclosure.” Is one not afraid that it will be said it is a tree which sheds its fruits<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The tree painted vermilion; one might come to profanely use its fruits.</i>? Did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.4"><i>Ševi‘it</i> Chapter 4, Notes 65–69</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 67a" href="/Shabbat.67a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 67a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 77b" href="/Chullin.77b"><i>Ḥulin</i> 77b</a>.</i>: “If a tree sheds its fruits, one colors it red with vermilion, loads it with stones, and frightens it into producing.” They said, there that it should not loosen its fruits, here that it should start to produce<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is copied from <i>Ševi‘it;</i> it makes no sense here. The correct answer is given in the next sentence.</i>. Rebbi Jonah asked: Why do we not teach “a line”, like the vermilion line as remembrance of the altar, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Middot 3:1" href="/Mishnah_Middot.3.1">Mishnah <i>Middot</i> 3:1</a>, describing the altar in the courtyard of the Temple. The blood of burnt offerings and animal purification offerings has to be poured on the upper wall of the altar; the blood of all other sacrifices goes on the lower part. The borderline is indicated by a red line, one cubit below the walkway around the altar. R. Jonah proposes to make the dedication mark narrow in contrast to the coloring of a misbehaving tree.</i>: “A vermilion line is like a belt in the middle to distinguish between upper and lower blood.” Rebbi Ḥiyya stated, one writes on it with vermilion: “dedicated.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 3:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.3.5.2"><i>Demay</i> 3:5, Notes 135–136</a>, where R. Yose states that one does not have to give tithes to save the nonobservant from sin. The statement of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel is in the Mishnah here.</i> Both Rebbi Yose and Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said the same thing. Just as Rebbi Yose said, we are not responsible for the dishonest, so Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, we are not responsible for the dishonest. It is reasonable that Rebbi Yose will agree with Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel; Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel will not agree with Rebbi Yose. Rebbi Yose will agree with Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel that we are not responsible for the dishonest. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel will not agree with Rebbi Yose, because it is not fitting for a <i>ḥaver</i> that anything not in order should leave his house<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">This is quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 32a" href="/Eruvin.32a">Babli (<i>Erubin</i> 32a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 9a" href="/Pesachim.9a"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 9a</a>) in the name of R. Ḥanina (there called R. Ḥanina from Khusistan.)</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following two paragraphs are also in <i>Šeqalim</i> 1:1, fol. 46a; <i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i> 1:1, fol. 80b/c. Similar arguments, in the name of different authorities, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Moed Katan 5a" href="/Moed_Katan.5a">Babli <i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i> 5a</a>. The discussion is about the note in the Mishnah that graves have to be marked.</i> From where about marks? Rebbi Berekhiah, Rebbi Jacob the son of the daughter of Jacob<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">A third generation Galilean Amora.</i>, in the name of Rebbi Onias from Hauran. Rebbi Yose said it in the name of Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Onias from Hauran. Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Uziel the son of Rebbi Onias from Hauran in the name of Rebbi Onias from Hauran (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:45" href="/Leviticus.13.45"><i>Lev.</i> 13:45</a>): “impure, impure, he shall call out;” the impurity itself has to call out and say to you: go away! Rebbi Hila in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 39:15" href="/Ezekiel.39.15"><i>Ez.</i> 39:15</a>): “The emissaries shall crisscross the land; if one sees a bone of a human he builds a sign near it.” [A <i>bone</i>,] from here that one makes signs for bones. A <i>human</i>, from here that one makes signs for spine and skull. <i>He builds</i>, from here that one makes signs on fixed stones. If you say on loose ones, it would move and make other places impure. <i>Near it</i>, on a place of purity. A <i>sign</i>, from here the marks.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shekalim (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Shekalim_(Lieberman).1.5">Tosephta <i>Šeqalim</i> 1:5</a>. This Tosephta follows the Yerushalmi, against the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Moed Katan 6a" href="/Moed_Katan.6a">Babli, <i>Moëd Qaṭan</i> 6a</a>, which reads “impure” in the first sentence.</i>: If one found a single marked stone, even though one should not keep it so, if somebody forms a tent over it he is pure; I say a compressed corpse<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">A corpse buried with its head between the legs, so it fitted under the stone. This is not a Jewish burial custom. If the body is not that of a Jew, a person standing over him and forming a “tent” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num</i>. 19</a>) with his body does not become impure (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 60b-61a" href="/Yevamot.60b-61a">Babli <i>Yebamot</i> 60b–61a</a>). If only one stone was needed, the corpse must be under it and cannot be Jewish since the previous <i>baraita</i> explained that the mark must be on a pure place. But then at least two markers are needed to define the impure spot.</i> was under it. If there were two, he who forms a tent over any one of them is pure; between them he is impure. If between them was a ploughed strip they are single stones, between them the area is pure and around them<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Any place the whitewash was splashed on the earth around the stone.</i> impure.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shekalim (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Shekalim_(Lieberman).1.5">Tosephta <i>Šeqalim</i> 1:5</a>, the reading is: One does not mark flesh or bones which do not induce tent-impurity.</i>: One does not mark flesh, for perhaps it will decompose<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Then it is no longer impure.</i>. Rebbi Justus bar Shunem asked before Rebbi Mana: Will that not cause pure food to be retroactively made impure<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">If the carrier of pure food learns that he has crossed a place where human flesh (without a bone) was buried.</i>? He said to him, it is better that these should become unusable for a limited time than that [the earth] become unusable forever.
The colleagues said, Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says it correctly, it is difficult for the rabbis! Does one not mark for the day, does he not steal in the night<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">What use is marking forbidden fruit if they are stolen in the dark of night.</i>? Rebbi Mana said to them, following what Rebbi Ḥanina<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 109a" href="/Sanhedrin.109a">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 109a</a>, the explanation is by the Babylonian Rava and the sermon by R. Yose the Tanna. In <i>Gen Rabba</i> 27, the explanation is anonymous, the sermon by R. Ḥanina.</i> said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 24:16" href="/Job.24.16"><i>Job</i> 24:16</a>): “He dug in the darkness under houses, by day they designated for them, they did not see light<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">In the masoretic text, לֹא יָדְעוּ אוֹר. The version לא ראו אור is in both Talmudim.</i>.” That is what the generation of the flood did, they were making signs with balsamum<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ὀποβάλσαμον, τό, “juice of the balsam-tree”.</i> resin and came to steal in the night. So did Rebbi Ḥanina preach in Sepphoris; there were 300 houses undermined.
Why can’t he mark<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last statement of the Mishnah, that discreet people in the Sabbatical put coins aside so that everybody may take the fourth-year fruits.</i>? If it [his vineyard] is marked, how can he be called discreet?
Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel parallels him who says “in the evening”, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 2:5" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).2.5">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 2:5</a>: “R. Jehudah says, in the morning the farmer has to say: Everything the poor did collect (ms. Erfurt: will collect) from the sheaves shall be abandoned; R. Dosa says, in the evening. But the Sages say, a declaration of abandonment under duress is not an abandonment, for we are not responsible for tricksters.” Since the rules for collecting single stalks are rather restrictive, RR. Jehudah and Dosa hold that the farmer should declare that anything the poor take in excess of what is legal shall be abandoned to them to protect the poor from sin. The Sages hold that we can only force all the poor to strictly follow the rules if they have to fear sinning when they overstep the legal bounds.<br>The parallel discussion to this paragraph is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 69" href="/Bava_Kamma.69">Babli <i>Baba Qama</i> 69 a/b</a>. There are major differences between Yerushalmi and Babli. The name tradition of the Tosephta is that of the Babli, the opposite of the Yerushalmi. In the text, the main (Vienna ms.) text, “will collect”, is the original reading of the Babli, the Erfurt text is the (conditionally) corrected reading of the Babli and the original reading of the Yerushalmi. The Babli (in the name of R. Joḥanan) rejects the notion that the “discreet religious ones” follow Rabban Simeon, the position implied by R. Joḥanan’s statement in the Yerushalmi.</i>: “Rebbi Dosa says, ‘everything the poor will collect between the sheaves shall be abandoned’, it is abandoned. Rebbi Jehudah says, in the evening. But the Sages say, a declaration of abandonment under duress is not an abandonment, for we are not responsible for tricksters.” But he is not parallel to him who says “in the morning”. Can one abandon anything if it is standing on the ground<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">“In the morning” means before the day’s harvest. Then the grain is still standing and no stalks lie on the ground that it should be possible to define what can be abandoned. Since the farmer does not want to abandon the field, his declaration is void.</i>? Rebbi Jeremiah said, it is even parallel to him who says “in the morning”. Is it not Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel’s<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 4:4:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.4.4.2-3">Chapter 4:6</a>. Even if the declaration by the farmer in the morning is legally invalid, it becomes activated once the stalks are taken up if we hold that the rules of Second Tithe and abandoned stalks are identical.</i>? In addition, he holds that taking Second Tithe is its redemption<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">He thinks that the entire declaration is unnecessary; but if somebody wants to do it out of religious scruples, he may put aside something of value for people to take.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, is that (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:19" href="/Leviticus.27.19"><i>Lev.</i> 27:19</a>): “It shall be his<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:19" href="/Leviticus.27.19"><i>Lev</i>. 27:19</a>): “If the person dedicating his field will redeem it, he shall add a fifth of its value and it shall be his.” This verse clearly ties the transfer of title to the property to the payment of money. If we hold that the rules of redemption are the same for dedicated property and Second Tithe, then taking up the tithe cannot be its redemption.</i>”? If somebody saw a loaf floating in a river and said, that loaf shall be dedicated, did he say anything<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since he cannot dispose of the loaf, he cannot dedicate it. Similarly, he cannot dedicate stalks to the poor if it is totally unknown which ones they will be.</i>? Rebbi Jeremiah said, before you ask all these questions regarding “in the morning”, ask them regarding “in the evening!” He answered him, he who holds “in the evening” does not have all these questions<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Since in the evening all grain has been cut for the day and bound in sheaves, it is now well-defined what is there for the poor and the owner can make any declaration he wishes. The same holds for the discreet religious ones who can redeem the well-defined fruits of their fourth-year vineyard or orchard.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> [Grapes from] a fourth-year vineyard have to be brought to Jerusalem from a distance up to a day’s march. What is this? Eilat from the South<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">An unknown place somewhere South of Hebron. One has to assume that the distance between Jerusalem and Eilat was approximately the same as the distance between Jerusalem and Aqrabeh, SE of Nablus.</i>, Aqrabeh from the North, Lydda from the West, the Jordan from the East. When the fruits increased<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">And ruined the trade in fruits on the markets in Jerusalem.</i> they decreed that they might be redeemed [even] close to the city wall. There was a condition made that anytime they decided, they could return to the previous situation<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Usually a rule imposed by a rabbinic assembly could be nullified or amended only by an assembly more prestigious than the first. This rule is explicitly waved in the present case.</i>. Rebbi Yose says, this condition was introduced when the Temple was destroyed, <i>viz</i>., that one would return to the previous situation if the Temple would be rebuilt soon, in our days<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Yose, no exemption for a future rabbinical assembly is created; the original assembly already fixed the terms for the disestablishment of the rule.</i>.<br><sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The entire Halakhah is from <i>Peah</i> 7:6; explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Maaser Sheni 5:3:9-5:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Maaser_Sheni.5.3.9-5.6">Notes 99–145</a>.</i> A fourth-year vineyard, the House of Shammai say, is not subject to a fifth and is not subject to removal; but the House of Hillel say, it is. The House of Shammai say, it is subject to single berries and gleanings and the poor redeem for themselves, but the House of Hillel say, all goes to the winepress.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Hila said, at the start they made wine in purity for Temple libations and grapes<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Eating grapes.</i> were rare. They decreed that it<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The yield of fourth-year vineyards.</i> had to be brought to Jerusalem from a distance up to a day’s march. They used to distribute it to relatives, neighbors, and acquaintances<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since the buyer knew that they were fourth-year grapes, the price would be depressed and the grapes could as well be distributed. (Explanation of S. Lieberman.) In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Maaser Sheni (Lieberman) 5:14" href="/Tosefta_Maaser_Sheni_(Lieberman).5.14">Tosephta, 5:14</a>, R. Simeon restricts the rule to vineyards in the sense of the formal definition (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 4:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.4.4.1">Mishnah <i>Kilaim</i> 4:6</a>); the yield of single vines should always be redeemed and the redemption money used under the rules of tithe money.</i>, and a small quantity adorned the market<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">This implies that in contrast to Second Tithe, the grapes from a fourth-year vineyard may be redeemed in Jerusalem proper, which makes the grapes profane and marketable. The reason given by R. Hila is attributed in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 5a" href="/Beitzah.5a">Babli (<i>Beẓah</i> 5a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rosh Hashanah 31b" href="/Rosh_Hashanah.31b"><i>Roš Haššanah</i> 31b</a>) to R. Joḥanan or R. Joshua ben Levi.</i>.
This disagrees with Nukai<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Since נקיי, together with מתיי, “Matthew”, appears as name of one of the disciples of Jesus, one might identify נקיי with Lucas by a change in liquids. The story is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Lamentations 3:9" href="/Lamentations.3.9"><i>Ekhâ Rabbati</i> on <i>Thr</i>. 3:9</a>; there, the person is only identified by his occupation, not by name. The following stories, except that of Mahalul, are also in that source.</i>. Nukai was community servant in Magdala of the dyers<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Probably a quarter of Magdala, N. of Tiberias. In <i>Ekhâ Rabbati</i>, the place is simply called Magdala.</i>. Every Sabbath eve, after preparing his candles<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">For the Friday night service in the synagoge. Latin <i>candela</i> “wax light, tallow candle, taper”.</i>, he went up, rested in the Temple, and went down to light them. Some say, he was the Torah reader. Every Sabbath eve he went up, prepared home.
The cap maker<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic طَر طُرر “pointed cap”. [Perhaps also cf. late Latin <i>triturator</i> “thresher”, <i>tritor</i> “grinder” (E. G.).</i> of Malul<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">In lower Galilee.</i> went up to observe the Sabbath in the Temple but nobody got up<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Sunday morning.</i> for his fig trees before him.
The daughters<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The unmarried women.</i> of Sepphoris went up, kept the Sabbath in the Temple, but nobody got up<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Sunday morning.</i> for their fig trees before them. The daughters of Lod were kneading their bread dough, went up, prayed, and descended before it became sour<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">It sems that in lod one never spoke Aramic. The parallel to the sentence in <i>Ekhâ Rabbati</i> also is in Hebrew.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Ekhâ Rabbati</i>, the story is quite different and not connected with instant travelling of long distances.</i> A person was ploughing. His cow broke away before him and started running. He ran and ran after her until he found himself in Babylonia. They asked him, when did you leave? He answered, today. They asked him, by which [road] did you come? He answered them, by this one, come and see it. He went out, wanted to see it, and did no longer recognize it.
